LIVE: Secretary of War Pete Hegseth speaks at the Americas Counter Cartel Conference

Department of War(297.0K) ·138:23 ·50.4K views · 2026-03-05
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of War)

TL;DR The Americas Counter-Cartel Conference marks a formal pivot from a law-enforcement-led drug strategy to a military-led offensive, establishing the "Donroe Doctrine" which treats drug cartels as foreign terrorist organizations and frames the Western Hemisphere as the...

TL;DR

The Americas Counter-Cartel Conference marks a formal pivot from a law-enforcement-led drug strategy to a military-led offensive, establishing the "Donroe Doctrine" which treats drug cartels as foreign terrorist organizations and frames the Western Hemisphere as the primary security perimeter for U.S. homeland defense.

The "Donroe Doctrine" and Hemispheric Defense

  • Strategic Reorientation: Secretary of War Pete Hegseth introduced the "Trump Corollary to the Monroe Doctrine" (or "Donroe Doctrine"), asserting that no external powers or non-state actors will be permitted to interfere with the peace and sovereignty of the Americas.
  • Greater North America: The Department of War has redefined its primary security perimeter as "Greater North America," encompassing all sovereign nations and territories north of the equator (from Greenland to Ecuador).
  • Quartersphere Defense: The administration is implementing a "quartersphere defense" model, focusing on high-intensity posture in the north and increased burden-sharing with partners in the south to protect the Atlantic and Pacific corridors.
  • Rejection of "Globalism": Speakers explicitly rejected "globalist" narratives and the concept of the "Global South," arguing that these frameworks erase national identities and weaken regional security.

Shift to Military-Led Offensive Operations

  • Combatting Narcoterroism: Stephen Miller and Secretary Hegseth categorized cartels as the "ISIS and Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere," arguing they cannot be solved through criminal justice alone but require "the tip of a very lethal spear."
  • Operation Southern Spear: Officials highlighted ongoing offensive operations focused on restoring deterrence by "finding, fixing, and finishing" cartel targets, including the sinking of narco-vessels.
  • Active Military Deployment: Secretary Hegseth confirmed that the 101st Airborne Division currently holds operational control of the U.S. southern border, shifting the mission from a law enforcement support role to a core military defense function.
  • Direct Action Evidence: The administration cited Operation Absolute Resolve—the January raid in Caracas to arrest indicted fugitives Nicolás Maduro and his wife—as proof of its willingness to use lethal force to dismantle threats.

Sovereignty, Borders, and Institutional Order

  • Border Centrality: The administration’s policy is rooted in the belief that sovereignty is impossible without total border control. Joseph Humire argued that by securing borders, "transnational" crime is reduced to a "local," solvable problem.
  • Critique of NGOs: Stephen Miller issued a sharp critique of non-governmental organizations, claiming they have historically pressured nations to adopt "soft" approaches that protected criminals over citizens.
  • The "Warrior Ethos": Hegseth emphasized a return to the "warrior ethos" within the Department of War, dismissing the phrase "diversity is our strength" as "the single dumbest phrase in military history," arguing instead that "unity is our strength."

Regional Partner Commitments and Results

  • El Salvador: Minister Merino credited the "Bukele model" for transforming the country from the world’s murder capital to the safest in the hemisphere by prioritizing the rights of "honest citizens" over "the wolf" (criminals).
  • Guatemala: Minister Saenz announced "Operation Belt of Fire" (Cinturón de Fuego), a major operational deployment to secure the porous border with Mexico against hybrid threats.
  • Jamaica: Ambassador Marks noted that U.S. partnership has already helped Jamaica reduce its murder rate by 40% over the past year.
  • Joint Security Declaration: All participating nations signed a declaration affirming a shared intent to expand bilateral and multilateral military cooperation to secure critical infrastructure and combat narcoterroism.

Notable Quotes

"The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations." — Stephen Miller, Assistant to the President for Homeland Security
"We are at long last putting America, Americans, and the Americas first... You can call it the Trump Corollary of the Monroe Doctrine, or for short, you can just call it the Donroe Doctrine." — Pete Hegseth, Secretary of War
"The West only achieved the place that it did in human history because it spent previous centuries eradicating the criminal elements within its territories... You have to establish order first." — Stephen Miller, Assistant to the President for Homeland Security
"He who forgives the wolf sacrifices the sheep. That was what was happening in our country... we decided to use the armed forces to combat the threat... and in a short time, we transformed El Salvador." — Minister Merino, Minister of Defense of El Salvador
Good morning. Welcome to the Americas Counter-Cartel Conference, hosted by the Secretary of War, the Honorable Pete Hegseth, and the Commander of the United States Southern Command, General Francis Donovan. We thank you for traveling to be here with us for this historic convening. The Americas Counter-Cartel Conference is a testament to our partnership and this administration's unwavering commitment to securing the US homelands and our hemisphere. At this time, I'd like to introduce the Commander of the United States Southern Command, General Francis Donovan. Buenos días. Welcome to Doral and the United States Southern Command for the Americas Counter-Cartel Conference. It was a pleasure to meet many of you last night at the reception, and even better to see many of the friends and teammates we met last month at the CHADS conference in Washington, D.C. Secretary Hegseth, sir, thank you for hosting this vital gathering and your enduring leadership. Each day you are ensuring the security, stability, and prosperity of the United States and the entire hemisphere. I'd also like to welcome and thank the Deputy Chief of Staff for Policy and Assistant to the President for Homeland Security, Mr. Stephen Miller. I also want to welcome the Under Secretary of War for Policy, the Honorable Eldridge Colby, who'll be joining us virtually today; the Chief of the National Guard Bureau, General Stephen Nordhaus—Spoc, thank you very much for being here; and the Assistant Secretary of War for Cyber Policy, the Honorable Catherine Sutton. I welcome the Acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas Security Affairs, and my now best friend, Mr. Joseph Humire; the Principal Deputy Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas Security Affairs, Mr. Mark Divisón; and the senior advisors to the Secretary of War, Mr. Erik Garessey, Mr. Patrick Weaver, and Mr. Philip Hegseth. Welcome most importantly to all of our partner nations' ministers, ambassadors, and leaders joining us today. Thank you all for being here. Today we stand together at a very critical moment. Narcotraffickers and drug cartels engage in campaigns of terror, violence, and corruption to enrich themselves and expand their insidious influence. They are poisoning and killing all of our nations' citizens. These sophisticated networks flood our streets with fentanyl, cocaine, and other drugs that kill at a higher rate than any conventional war. This is the single gravest threat to security in our region and across our hemisphere. To counter this threat, the United States is guided by the strategic vision of peace through security, set forth in both the United States National Security Strategy and the National Defense Strategy. These documents give our military a clear mandate in the Western Hemisphere to defend the US homeland and increase burden-sharing with our allies and partners to do so. We are your premier partner to work with, by, with, and through your nations to achieve shared objectives. But when necessary, we will not hesitate to act. Operation Southern Spear and Absolute Resolve are proof of this commitment. Through these efforts, the United States is combating narcoterrorism and drug trafficking and, most importantly, restoring deterrence. The Western Hemisphere is our neighborhood, and the narcoterrorists who seek to export drugs and death to our shores and your shores will be dismantled and defeated. As allies and partners, we must take aggressive action together, sharing the burden through cooperation. We now call upon our long-standing relationships, built on a foundation of trust and linked through shared culture, history, and economic aspirations, to unite for the benefit of all of our nations. Our mutual security is at stake. Through combined operations, training exercises, and intelligence and information sharing, we will achieve greater domain awareness, interoperability, and capacity to strengthen regional security. With all nations shouldering this burden, our collective resilience will prevent shared adversaries from using the region to further their malign agendas. The time is now. Today marks the next step towards moving out to deter and defeat those who threaten the security, stability, and prosperity of the United States and this hemisphere. We all face these threats and the most effective way to counter and eradicate them is by standing together. Before I turn the stage to our Mr. Humire, I'd like to once again thank you for your stalwart partnership. Your participation here sends an unmistakable message to partners and adversaries alike. We are taking the fight to the narcoterrorists and the cartels. Not only are we redoubling our commitment to defend the sovereignty and security of our nations, we are doing it together to realize our common vision of a safer, stronger, and more prosperous Western Hemisphere. Let's get to work. Thank you, General Donovan. Ladies and gentlemen, performing the duties of Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and Americas Security Affairs, Joseph Humire. Good morning, Buenos días. And I'd be remiss if I didn't begin by thanking my new best friend General Donovan for your excellent remarks and frankly for your excellent teamwork here at SOUTHCOM. Your team has truly been amazing at putting all this together in just a matter of a few weeks. So thank you, General. I'd also like to begin by acknowledging a special team of senior advisors and defense officials who if not for their efforts, and not just today but over the last several months, we frankly just wouldn't be here today. So a special thank you to Principal Deputy Assistant Secretary Divisón, Acting Deputy Assistant Secretary Strasba, Mr. Erik Garessey, Senior Advisor Matteo Heydar, and a special sincere and heartfelt thank you to senior advisors Philip Hegseth and Patrick Weaver. If it wasn't for you, frankly this would not have happened. To all our interagency friends and foreign partners, this is truly a historic moment for the United States, for the Western Hemisphere, and for the world. In the room today, we have senior leadership from the Department of State, Department of Homeland Security, National Security Council, Homeland Security Council, Office of National Drug Control Policy, Office of the Vice President, and of course the White House. I'm honored to be here with you today as Secretary Hegseth's Acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas. Welcome everyone to the first Americas Counter-Cartel Conference in history. Every morning before I enter the Pentagon, I say a prayer in my car to our Heavenly Father. And I do this because I'm blessed. I'm blessed to be able to work in a once-in-a-lifetime administration, to work for a once-in-a-generation leader, President Donald J. Trump. And frankly, if it wasn't for President Trump, none of us, including our foreign partners, would be here today. President Trump, Secretary Hegseth, and Mr. Stephen Miller understand an empirical truth that all the leadership of the United States missed for decades, if not centuries. They missed the fact that the Americas is an extension of US homeland defense, that hemispheric defense is a part of US homeland defense. And this statement of fact is now reflected in US policy through the 2025 National Security Strategy and the 2026 National Defense Strategy that correctly places the Americas as a regional priority. Just think about where we were a little over one year ago today. Those of you that know me know that I've been working and studying on regional issues for more than 20 years. So I can confidently say that in 2024, prior to President Trump, I had never seen the Americas in worse shape. Prior to President Trump, there was record-breaking illegal mass migration that was collapsing our border and many of your borders as well. There were record-breaking deaths of Americans from deadly drugs that were transiting through your countries and stealing your sovereignty. The explosion of transnational organized crime in every single country throughout the Americas was destroying our shared region. If you think about it, before President Trump, we were breaking all the records for all the wrong things. And it was even worse because there was a growing false narrative throughout the region that was taking hold that said you cannot win a war against major cartels and gangs because they are too powerful. In some cases, and even in some countries, frankly they were giving up and adopted a posture of "if you can't beat them, join them." That's not tolerable anymore. Not for the United States, not for your countries, and not for our neighborhood. If you look to your right and you look to your left, you will find partners that are willing to fight with you. And if we collectively fight together, there is no gang, there is no terrorist, there is no cartel that is more powerful than us. We are here today because each of you has committed to doing your part. And that commitment cannot just be lip service. This must come with a new approach and a new normal to dismantle and destroy our shared threats of cartels and narcoterrorism. There'll be a lot of discussions today on a myriad of topics, but I really want to quickly emphasize two that are central to this effort: border security and deterrence. If you do not control your borders, you do not have a country. Border security and territorial integrity, as President Trump has shown, is possible. Since 1648, since the Peace of Westphalia, sovereignty has been the bedrock of human flourishing. You do not have a democracy if you do not have your sovereignty, and you cannot have sovereignty without borders. Open borders make transnational organized crime fundamentally transnational. And you do not combat transnational organized crime with transnational institutions; you fight it with borders and national sovereignty. If we remove the transnational from transnational organized crime, it becomes a local problem; it becomes a solvable problem. The second point is on deterrence. Interdiction, while necessary, is not deterrence. Dialogue, while useful, is not deterrence. Failing to control your prisons, your borders, or your waters is not deterrence. Going on offense is deterrence. Operation Southern Spear shows that deterrence is taking lethal and bold actions. And our goal is for every partner in this room today to start leading deterrence-focused offensive operations against your cartels. And finally, to wrap up, I want to make a point about geography. Geography in this hemisphere is indispensable to security. I mean, the secret sauce of Latin America and the Caribbean to be a regional priority for the United States is not the size of your trade, it's not your populations or your military. It's because we are always going to be connected because of our shared geography. The United States understood this going back to 1815, when two leaders in a relatively young Department of War pushed back against their own establishment at that time, not to look north or to look east, but rather to look south. The Department of War back then fought and won the Battle of New Orleans in 1815 that connected the United States to what is now the Gulf of America. And that moment 210 years ago connected my country to yours. Just a few years after that, these same two leaders led the Department of War to fight another war on our southern border, the Seminole Wars, this time to protect our border against tribes that trespassed our land, raided our towns, and killed Americans. We ultimately won these battles and transferred the great state of Florida in 1821. And then a mere two years later, one of these leaders declared something called the Monroe Doctrine. The legacy of then-Secretary of War James Monroe, who later became President James Monroe, and Major General Andrew Jackson, who also later became President, defines the challenge that we have before us today. They say history doesn't repeat itself, but it tends to rhyme. And our interest over this shared geography is the same today as it was 200 years ago. Today, the Department of War once again has historic leadership under Secretary Pete Hegseth, committed to working with all of you to secure the Western Hemisphere once and for all. Thank you, God bless you. Thank you, General Donovan. I would now like to invite Deputy Chief of Staff for Policy and Assistant to the President for Homeland Security Stephen Miller to the podium. Sir, the floor is yours. Thank you, General Donovan, and General Nordhaus. And thank you to my good friend Secretary Hegseth, whom I've long admired but I've gotten to know personally in the last number of months. He is as fine a gentleman and as fierce a warrior as you will ever meet. We've all learned to put our faith and confidence in his leadership of the War Department, so thank you, Pete. There's been a paradigm shift under President Trump in the Western Hemisphere in the last 14 months. Some of that was touched on by the earlier speakers, but just to restate it: We are not going to cede an inch of territory in this hemisphere to our enemies or our adversaries. Our national security, our homeland security, and the safety and well-being of our people begins at home, begins in our neighborhood, and begins in our home region. For too long, we have allowed foreign enemies, foreign adversaries, and enemy terrorist organizations to control territory and spaces in this hemisphere. There, they can project power, project threats, and directly threaten the lives of your citizens and our citizens. And so under the leadership of President Trump, we are using hard power, military power, and lethal force to protect and defend the American homeland. Not a single one of your nations should tolerate the existence of a single square mile of territory that is under the control of any entity other than the sovereign governments of your country. The idea that we would have in this hemisphere areas that are under the physical control of foreign terrorist organizations or paramilitary organizations is completely unacceptable. The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations. What we have learned after decades of effort is that there is not a criminal justice solution to the cartel problem. There are elements of the problem that require a criminal justice solution to be sure. But just as we fought Al-Qaeda and fought ISIS with the tip of a very lethal spear, the reason why this is a conference with military leadership and not a conference of lawyers is because these organizations can only be defeated with military power. I see some heads nodding up front because they understand you're dealing with a lot of lawyers in your own country, I'm sure. You have my permission not to listen to them. The key condition of human flourishing, of prosperity, is order and security. A country cannot pursue economic growth, cultural enrichment, art, architecture, education, or science if it doesn't have fundamental security first. For too long, we've been caught in the vicious cycle in this hemisphere where countries from Mexico down to the tip of South America do not provide their citizens with basic physical security. So those citizens, in search of economic opportunity, illegally immigrate to the United States. They pay the smugglers and pay the cartels, who then grow richer and grow more powerful. Then your countries in turn become poor because you've lost your citizens and your human capital. You've lost your opportunities to develop and to grow. You get poor, the cartels get more powerful, nothing changes, and nothing improves. You have to break that cycle. You have to completely sever it. And that begins by dismantling these organizations that deal drugs, deal violence, assassinate, extort, blackmail, and then also traffic illegally in human beings. That is a form of terrorism, too. Illegal immigration facilitated by criminal networks is a form of terrorism. Those who are stealing your children and stealing your families and stealing your human capital are part of that terrorist continuum and should be thought of as such. It is not a victimless crime when people are paying human smuggling organizations, because that money then fuels assassinations, it fuels violence, and it fuels instability. For decades, a lot of organizations were sent to your countries—these are known as NGOs, I think you know the term non-governmental organizations—and they gave you very, very bad advice for a very, very long time. Basically they told you, in short, that you needed to be softer and gentler and nicer to the criminals. They said you had to demilitarize your approach and extend more rights and more opportunities to the people who were sabotaging and destroying your society. I would make the point to you, as others have pointed out before, including the President of El Salvador, that the West only achieved the place that it did in human history because it spent previous centuries eradicating the criminal elements within its territories. So the West that we came to know, that had the great music and architecture and science and the most powerful economy, it had to spend centuries previous to that establishing order first. By the time you get to, say, 18th century England or 19th century England, there had been centuries of previous history in which ruthless means were used to get rid of the people who were raping and murdering and defying established systems of order and justice. When people could live freely and peaceably and children and families and women could be safe, then it became possible to pursue all of these higher elements of life. So when people who are coming to countries like yours that have had struggles and difficulties and pains and challenges are telling you anything other than "first and foremost secure your countries and your territory," they're giving you not only terrible advice, but advice that would have prevented Europe from becoming Europe. The reason why many Western countries are struggling today is they've forgotten the eternal truths and wisdoms they once followed. And that's why here under President Trump and in America we are returning to those fundamental truths; we're returning to those fundamental principles. So I'll just close by saying that you will have in President Trump, in the White House, in the War Department, the Department of State, and the whole of our government, unwavering support, unwavering commitment, and fidelity in achieving this mission. That mission is very simply to eradicate the criminal cartels and terrorist organizations, to deprive them of control of weapons, spaces, institutions, and territory. And the human rights that we are going to protect are not of the savages that torture and rape and murder. The human rights that we are going to protect are of the peaceable citizens who have an absolute human right to live in physical safety and security every day of their lives. And in that mission, I promise we will be your closest partner. Thank you. Ladies and gentlemen, it is now my distinct pleasure to introduce our Secretary of War, the Honorable Pete Hegseth. Well, good morning everybody. Well, General Donovan and I are pleased to welcome all of you to Southern Command this morning. I'm honored to be the first Secretary of War to lead a hemispheric defense conference like this in more than 30 years. This conference is about you. This conference is about us. This conference is not called the "America's Counter-Cartel Conference"—apostrophe S—it's the Americas Counter-Cartel Conference. This is about what we together can do. It's been more than 200 years since our nations struggled for independence from foreign rule. The War of 1812 was about the United States defending its independence and protecting its territory. A 47-year-old Carolinian who was a major general in the army, as was mentioned, Andrew Jackson, defeated a British army almost double the size. Addressing his troops after victory at the Battle of New Orleans, Jackson declared, "Natives of different states acting together for the first time have reaped the fruits of an honorable union." His success galvanized a young country. In 1816, our country elected a revolutionary war hero as president. James Monroe fought in the Continental Army. Leading a charge against German mercenaries fighting for the British, the enemy fired a musket ball at him. It severed his artery and he almost died. But the young captain rallied and grew to be governor of Virginia, Secretary of War, and one of our greatest presidents. In 1823, President Monroe said that the United States owed the success of that young country to the candor and amicable relations with other powers to affirm our national policy. President Monroe declared, "We should consider any attempt on malign countries to extend their system to any portion of this hemisphere as dangerous to our peace and safety." It was the Monroe Doctrine of 1823. Today, some 200 years later, we still marvel at the wisdom of President Monroe's declaration. We, like you, want borders and sovereign territories that are secure. We want unfettered access to key terrain and trade so that our nations can industrialize. And we want to prevent external powers from threatening our peace and independence in our shared neighborhood. This is the essence of the Monroe Doctrine. No external power will interfere in this hemisphere. Ours should be a region of strong, sovereign nations. It's the same principle that animates President Trump's defense approach today. President Trump and his War Department are ushering in a new era of homeland and hemispheric defense, rooted in the unique heritage of our nation and your nations. Under President Trump, we restored the Department of War. You see, the Department of War was established by General George Washington and served our nation from its founding through our hard-fought victory in World War II. Many of your countries also once had ministries of war. Under President Trump, our nation has a newfound resolve that our founders would recognize. As Stephen alluded to and spoke to, they would recognize it, and they would appreciate it. We are at long last putting America, Americans, and the Americas first, ensuring peace through strength and restoring common sense. At our department, we're doing it by restoring the warrior ethos, rebuilding our military to historic levels, and re-establishing deterrence in this hemisphere and around the world. For too long, leaders in Washington and their foreign policy functionaries abandoned the simple wisdom of the Monroe Doctrine. They were consumed with the United States only going abroad. They ignored the warnings of President John Quincy Adams who said that if our nation went overseas only in search of monsters to destroy, that we would cease to be the ruler of our own spirit and our own backyard. Under previous leaders, we grew obsessed with every other theater and every other border in the world except our own. These elites reduced our power and presence in this hemisphere, opting for a benign neglect that was anything but benign. In your countries, many leaders accepted the status quo to coexist with narcoterrorism, or for a law enforcement-alone approach that failed to deter and dismantle threats. Same happened in our country under the previous administration. And the result of this collective neglect was fatal. More than 1 million Americans were killed by fentanyl, cocaine, and other drugs. Numbers greater than any casualties we've sustained in war. Millions of illegal aliens invaded our borders and destabilized nations across the hemisphere. Under Joe Biden's administration, the human smuggling industry exploded by more than 2,000 percent, from a 500 million dollar so-called industry in 2018 to 13 billion by 2022. Cartels of terrorists across our hemisphere enabled by adversaries created and profited from chaos. What creates chaos? No leadership creates chaos. In our nation, narcoterrorist groups like Tren de Aragua prospered. They raped, they poisoned, they murdered innocent Americans. They terrorized our cities, our towns, and our neighborhoods, like they do yours. Across this region, these gangsters control and terrorize large swaths of your territories, kill thousands of your people, and promote chaos that makes normal life sometimes almost impossible. Crime has spiked massively. This, our hemisphere, has one-eighth of the population, yet one-third of the violent crime. There's a reason for that. America's retreat from protecting its citizens and the complacency of many neighbors in this hemisphere was a great betrayal. A great betrayal against our people and our nations. While President Trump recognizes the wisdom of the Monroe Doctrine, the days of us betraying and endangering our own citizens are finished. President Trump has re-established the Monroe Doctrine, the Trump Corollary of the Monroe Doctrine, or if you'd like, for short, you can just call it the Donroe Doctrine. Under President Trump, securing America's interest in the Western Hemisphere and keeping our homeland safe are our top national security priorities. The President's historic National Defense Strategy ensures that the Department of War will prioritize resources around the threats and objectives that are core to homeland defense and the prosperity of the American people. To start, the Department of War is once again defending our borders as a priority of our national defense. With our law enforcement partners, we are defending our borders and making our streets safe again. Crossings at our southern border, at the US-Mexico border, have dropped to the lowest number in history. Murders nationwide have plummeted by more than 20 percent. We now have operational control of our southern border. Zero people are crossing our southern border. We will not rest until we have complete operational control of every inch of all of our borders. So it starts with us, we're addressing it, but we're not stopping there. Under President Trump, for the first time in history, the Department of War is on the offense against narcoterrorists as well. I should back up a step, though. In order to get 100 percent operational control of our border, we didn't just look at border patrol and say that's your job, it's a law enforcement function. We deployed the United States military. Right now, the division command of the 101st Airborne is in operational control of the southern border. We ensured that the most capable assets we had in our country were deployed to our own border to defend that border against the narcoterrorists and drug dealers seeking to enter our country. We are not treating it simply as a law enforcement function. Working with our partners, but recognizing it's a core military function. Going on offense with Operation Southern Spear has restored deterrence against the narcoterrorist cartels that profited from poisoning Americans. Last month, we went a few weeks without targeting a single boat. Why? Well, because we couldn't find a whole lot of boats to sink. And that's the whole point: to establish deterrence from narcoterrorists who have been able to traffic almost unfettered. If the consequence was simply to be arrested and then released, well, that's a consequence they'd already priced in a long time ago. A new dynamic had to be put in motion in order for their decision cycle to change. When adversaries are forced to move out of comfortable positions, as you know as military men and women, they're more vulnerable when they're moving in a different direction. Which is also the point. Drug overdoses that kill our citizens have dropped. Flows of fentanyl, a weapon of mass destruction, are down 56 percent. We will continue to destroy these threats. In January, US forces raided and arrested the most dangerous house in the most dangerous compound inside the most fortified fort in the capital city of Caracas. They targeted two indicted individuals, Nicolás Maduro and his wife, fugitives of US law. Operation Absolute Resolve showed the bravery of America's best, our warfighters. Nobody can do what the United States of America can do. It's not even close. President Trump has shown what is possible when you reject the status quo delusion that threats to our homeland and our hemisphere are somehow secondary. America is prepared to take on these threats and go on the offense alone if necessary. However, it is our preference, and it is the goal of this conference, that in the interest of this neighborhood, we all do it together with you, with our neighbors and with our allies who are eager and willing and capable. To work together, we must first acknowledge what was lost and then understand what needs to be restored. All the nations represented in this room are offsprings of Western civilization. Our nations are and always will be united by our heritage, our history, and geography in this new world. We share the same interests. And because of this, we face an essential test: whether our nations will be and remain Western nations, with distinct characteristics, Christian nations, under God, proud of our shared heritage, with strong borders and prosperous people. We must be ruled not by violence and chaos, but by law, order, and common sense. Or whether we are permanently torn apart by something else, led astray by competing forces. Radical narcocommunism and anarcho-tyranny threaten our people, borders and sovereign lands in the name of a false sovereignty or a false peace. Uncontrolled mass migration overwhelms domestic resources intended for deserving citizens and drives unchecked crime and unchecked violence. There is also the belief in so-called globalism that seeks to erase our distinct national identities in the name of tolerance. It seeks to erase our borders in the name of compassion, and erase our warrior ethos and that which makes us strong in the name of so-called diversity and political correctness. One of the dumbest phrases in military history, which used to be echoed from the former Defense Department in the previous administration, was "our diversity is our strength." They said it over and over and over again. It's the single dumbest phrase in military history. Because what is our strength? Our strength is our unity. Our strength is in our shared purpose, in the training we do together, and the capabilities we have. In our context, it is the Constitution we swear an oath to defend together and the uniform we wear together. That is our strength. We reject other notions and offer a new choice. We must defend our national interest and restore our national identities. Otherwise, the outcome is the same. Our hemisphere will be poorer, more dangerous, and weaker. This matters not because we seek to preach to you about culture wars or ideologies, or to tell you how to run your countries. We're not here to tell you how to run your countries. Instead, it matters how we assess the threats facing us and the choices that we make on how to overcome them. That is what will define our destiny. President Trump understands that today's threats to border security and key terrain in our hemisphere are existential questions for our nation and for all of yours. When adversaries conduct incursions in this hemisphere, off the coast of a US state, Alaska, or off the coast of Greenland, or in the Gulf of America or the Caribbean, that is a direct threat to the United States homeland and to peace in this hemisphere. When adversaries control ports or infrastructure along strategic choke points for US and hemispheric trade, such as the Panama Canal, or install military facilities just miles off our shore, that is a threat to the United States homeland and peace to this hemisphere. When terrorist killers and cartels capture strategic infrastructure, resources and entire towns or cities close to US borders and US shores, or profit from mass illegal migration, that is a threat to the United States homeland and a threat to all of you as well. To the Americas: The same adversaries that threaten our shared heritage threaten our shared geography as well. They seek to displace the historic north-south relationship that we've always shared with some sort of a new "global south" that excludes the United States and other Western nations. But it includes non-Western powers and other adversaries. The answer to our challenge is not to ignore our geography in the name of global interests, but to embrace our shared geography in the name of national interests. That is why President Trump has drawn a new strategic map from Greenland to the Gulf of America to the Panama Canal and its surrounding countries. At the Department of War, we call this strategic map the Greater North America. Why? Because every sovereign nation and territory north of the Equator, from Greenland to Ecuador and from Alaska to Guyana, is not part of the global south. It is our immediate security perimeter in this great neighborhood that we all live in. Each one of these countries border either the North Atlantic or the North Pacific. Each one of these countries sits north of the two basic geographic barriers that exist in this region: the Amazon and the Andes Mountains. This is basic geography that we don't teach in schools as much as we should. It restores our north-south relations, and we must get it right. In the north, the United States must enhance posture and presence in cooperation with you and our sovereign partners to defend our shared immediate security perimeter. In the south, meaning south of the equator, the other side of this great neighborhood, we will strengthen partnerships through increased burden-sharing. This will enable you to take a greater role to defend the South Atlantic and the South Pacific and to secure critical infrastructure and resources. We will do this in partnership with us and other Western nations. This is what we did in World War II, just like we sunk ships with torpedoes in World War II. At the Department of War, we call it the quartersphere defense. And we will do this again. If we're serious about our national security and if we prioritize geography, business as usual will not stand. This means that for every country in this hemisphere, border security must be your top priority. Border security is national security. President Trump said it when he first started to run: you're not a country if you don't have borders, and he's right. President Trump sealed our border by directing the military to mobilize resources and personnel to stop invasion by cartels and other criminal actors. You can do this as well. And many of you have. This also means that you too can and must go on the offense against narcoterrorists. We've had great collaboration with many countries in recent months. I want to thank every country in this room that has supported our access to find, fix, and finish cartel targets. We have only just begun to work with you. More must be done by you and by us to target narcoterrorist groups across all domains. We will dismantle narcoterrorist networks in this hemisphere and we will deny access to the state adversaries who support them. We will correct the mistakes of the past, the mistakes of the so-called global south. I want to thank General Donovan who has been an excellent partner and a great new leader here at Southern Command. One of the great mistakes of the past was that our leaders didn't provide the US Southern Command with the support that it needed. It was a command chock-full of lawyers and social workers and NGOs and law enforcement. That's changing. General, I'm directing my team to correct these historic failures, to ensure that your command has the resources and staff that you need for this robust mission ahead. It's the least we can do. I want to absolutely—thank you. It's about time. But it's not just us at the Department of War and it's not just Southern Command; this is across the Trump administration. Stephen Miller, thank you for your support and your leadership, for your clarity. Thank you for understanding why this matters to each American citizen and why the War Department is central to ensuring that it happens. It doesn't happen without President Trump and it doesn't happen without your clarity and your leadership. I want to thank General Nordhaus as well for your support at every level. There are so many to thank. I see Corey Lewandowski here from the Department of Homeland Security; it doesn't happen without the Department of Homeland Security. Of course, Joseph Humire, the other part of the new bromance between him and General Donovan. Erik Garessey, who from day one in my office—he was my first sergeant in Iraq and is one of my senior advisors—said we need to be focused on the cartel threat. He worked at SOUTHCOM for a long time and built the team to help put us in that direction, a lot of which manifests to this day. Patrick Weaver, Phil Hegseth—whom I happen to know—and Ricky Bria, my chief, thank you for doing a great job putting this together. Tony Salisbury, thank you. So many folks that every single day in Washington, I hope you know, are working hard to run as fast as you are to stand alongside you in this mission. This is not a conference with flags so we can pat ourselves on the back. I can tell you that if I sold that to President Trump as the objective, he'd kick me out of his office. This is an operational conference to bring our countries more closely together to achieve a shared objective and do so aggressively. This is not a one-way street. Every partner in this region has to do more and invest more in your security as well. We, like you, want a hemisphere of sovereign, secure and prosperous nations. We, like you, want what another great American president, Teddy Roosevelt, called the permanent peace on this hemisphere. And it will require action from all of us. You know, our schools used to teach American schoolchildren about our blessed history. Every child in grade school grew up knowing the remarkable story of this country. They knew that President James Monroe was once an orphan, that at a mere 16 years old he had to look after his siblings. I've got an almost 16-year-old; he couldn't look after anything. It's remarkable. Well, that orphan grew to be not just one of the greatest presidents, but one of our greatest Americans. After his presidency, when his wife died and he became frail and ill with heart failure, he still spoke about what he called our shared cause of liberty. He still talked about the toils and the perils of our war for independence. James Monroe, our fifth president, died on July 4th, the 4th of July, Independence Day, 1831—50 years after 1776. What I like to say is here at the Department of War, we're in the 1775 business, which is when Americans took up arms before they even declared independence. All of you are also in the 1775 business. Without the force of arms, without our militaries, we cannot keep our countries safe. That's my responsibility, and it is yours. With Donald Trump in the Oval Office and with all of you here, we can still realize that long-ago dream of James Monroe in our time. We will make the Americas great again. Thank you and God bless you. Thank you, Secretary Hegseth. We now invite each of our partners to provide short remarks. Please deliver your remarks seated at your chair instead of coming up to the podium. Prime Minister Phillips of Guyana, you have the floor. Thank you. I thank the government of the United States of America and the Honorable Secretary of War Pete Hegseth for convening this inaugural Americas Counter-Cartel Conference and for the invitation extended to Guyana. Guyana and the United States share a strong and expanding strategic partnership built on mutual trust and robust defense and security cooperation. Through capacity building, joint exercises, and policy and technical engagements, our countries have worked closely together to combat narcotrafficking and other forms of transnational crime. The transnational activities of cartels, gangs, and other criminal actors threaten regional peace and security. These networks undermine the rule of law, weaken institutions, and threaten the safety and economic well-being of our citizens. For countries like Guyana, located along important maritime and regional transit corridors, addressing narcotrafficking and narcoterrorism requires strong cooperation among our nations. These threats demand coordinated intelligence sharing, enhanced operational cooperation, and strengthened defense capabilities across the hemisphere. We must continue to respect the sovereignty and territorial integrity of every state. As our region continues to grow economically and strategically, it is also essential that we work together to protect critical infrastructure, strengthen national institutions, and enhance our resilience to evolving security threats. Through deeper cooperation and partnership, we can ensure that our hemisphere remains safe, secure, and a true zone of peace. Guyana welcomes the adoption of the Joint Security Declaration of this conference and looks forward to joining the coalition to combat narcoterrorism. The government of Guyana remains committed to being a reliable and active partner in advancing the security and stability of the Western Hemisphere. Thank you very much. Thank you, Mr. Prime Minister. Lieutenant General Presti of Argentina, you have the floor. Señor Secretario de Guerra, señor General, autoridades aquí presentes. Muchas gracias. Thank you, sir. Minister Munroe of the Bahamas, you have the floor. Good morning, excellencies. On behalf of the government and people of the Commonwealth of the Bahamas, I extend warm appreciation to Secretary Hegseth and the government of the United States for convening this conference and for the seriousness of purpose which it signals to criminal networks across this hemisphere. Let me be direct: this gathering is a declaration that we will no longer permit cartel networks, narcotics traffickers, and organized criminal enterprises to exploit the gaps between and within our jurisdictions. And the Bahamas stands fully committed to that declaration. Allow me to use this occasion to reiterate that the military cooperation between the Bahamas and the United States must be based on the shared commitment to peace, sovereignty, and security. This must be consistent with our existing bilateral agreements and with full respect for sovereignty and laws of each country and with due regard to the international legal norms. The Bahamas intends to work closely with the United States Department of War in expanding joint military cooperation, recognizing the shared responsibility for regional security and stability. The Bahamas welcomes closer collaboration on military-to-military efforts regarding border security, counter-narcotics, interoperability, and intelligence sharing. The disparity between the scale of our security responsibility and our resource capacity is significant, and it is not sustainable. Our vast maritime domain requires continuous aerial and maritime patrols, our surveillance infrastructure requires sustained capital investment, and our security personnel require advanced training in counter-narcotics, counter-trafficking, and cyber defense. The concept of peace through strength carries enduring resonance, and if we are candid, enduring misapplications. For a small state like the Bahamas, it is not about arsenal size; it is about integrity of our institutions, the credibility of our courts, and the depths of our partnerships. Hence, peace through strength as we understand it is not unilateral. It is collective. It rests on the recognition that hemispheric security is indivisible—that what weakens Nassau ultimately weakens Miami, that instability in Port-au-Prince reaches Nassau long before it reaches Washington. Small states on the front line of transnational crime deserve genuine partners, not conditional patrons. Let me end by stating the Bahamas' commitment and expectation with directness this forum warrants. The Bahamas will continue to operate as a front-line maritime security partner, disrupting narcotics trafficking routes, denying criminal networks the use of our waters and airspace, and contributing meaningfully to the security architecture of this hemisphere. The Bahamas brings to this partnership something no defense budget can manufacture: geography, institutional commitment, and a track record of cooperation spanning more than four decades. We ask only what we give: genuine partnership grounded in mutual respect and shared responsibility. The Americas either secures itself collectively or it fails collectively. The Commonwealth of the Bahamas has chosen collective security, and we invite every partner assembled here to meet that commitment with equal resolve, equal investment, and equal determination. Thank you. Thank you, Minister Munroe. Minister Marin of Belize, you have the floor. Good morning. Secretary Hegseth, General Donovan, distinguished ministers, heads of delegation, and colleagues. On behalf of the government and people of Belize, I extend sincere appreciation to the United States for convening this timely and important conference here in Miami. I thank the leadership of the Department of War for bringing us together around a shared purpose: strengthening our collective response to cartel activity and the transnational crime across the Western Hemisphere. For Belize, this is not abstract. As a small but strategically located nation bridging Central America and the Caribbean, we sit along key maritime and overland routes exploited by criminal networks. These networks traffic narcotics, weapons, and human beings. They undermine governance, distort economies, fuel corruption, and erode the safety and confidence of our citizens. Cartels and transnational criminal organizations do not respect our borders. They adapt quickly, leveraging technology, exploiting institutional gaps, and moving seamlessly across jurisdictions. Our response therefore must be equally adaptive, coordinated, and resolute. Belize remains firmly committed to a comprehensive security strategy that combines strong border protection, maritime domain awareness, intelligence-led operations, and close interagency collaboration. We are investing in the professionalization of our defense and security forces, strengthening our legal frameworks, and enhancing cooperation with regional and international partners. But no nation, in particular Belize, can confront this threat alone. The Western Hemisphere is interconnected by trade, travel, and shared democratic values. When criminal networks destabilize one country, the effects ripple across us all. That is why partnerships, regional and multilateral, are not optional; they are essential. We must deepen intelligence sharing, improve joint operational capabilities, strengthen financial tracking to disrupt illicit flows, and invest in community resilience to prevent recruitment by criminal organizations. We must ensure that our efforts uphold the rule of law, for it is precisely this principle that criminal enterprises seek to weaken. Colleagues, Belize stands ready to work shoulder to shoulder with our partners to safeguard our region. Together, through unity of purpose and action, we can deny transnational criminal organizations the space they seek to operate and build a more secure and prosperous hemisphere for all our peoples. El Perú prioriza la sólida relación que mantiene con los Estados Unidos en materia de seguridad y defensa. Esta es una prioridad que se fortalece día a día. El Perú está firmemente comprometido con la lucha contra las organizaciones criminales transnacionales. Este es un fenómeno complejo y multidimensional. El tráfico de drogas, el narcoterrorismo, el tráfico ilícito de armas, la trata de personas, y la minería ilegal operan con estructuras que trascienden fronteras. Utilizan tecnologías emergentes y buscan desestabilizar la institucionalidad democrática. Estas amenazas se proyectan simultáneamente en los dominios terrestre, marítimo, aéreo y ciberespacio, y esto requiere respuestas integrales y coordinadas. Para el Perú, la lucha contra el narcotráfico constituye una prioridad estratégica, dado que somos uno de los principales productores mundiales de hoja de coca y cocaína. Tenemos el impacto de estas economías ilícitas contra la seguridad ciudadana, el medio ambiente y las poblaciones más vulnerables. Nuestra experiencia histórica ha mostrado la estrecha vinculación que existe entre el terrorismo y el tráfico de drogas. De igual manera, reconocemos que el ciberdelito constituye una amenaza transnacional que solo puede ser enfrentada con una cooperación sostenida y mecanismos internacionales efectivos. El Perú ha trabajado activamente en la negociación y suscripción de la Convención de la ONU contra el Ciberdelito, comprometiéndose a prevenir y sancionar estas conductas, así como a fortalecer los mecanismos de cooperación internacional contra las amenazas digitales emergentes. Con este entorno estratégico, necesitamos ser más interoperables, intercambiar información, mejorar nuestras capacidades y tener confianza mutua y respeto a la soberanía de los Estados. Y solo quiero terminar diciéndoles que reafirmamos nuestro compromiso con una acción coordinada que permitirá preservar la estabilidad regional y garantizar un hemisferio más estable y próspero para nuestras naciones. Gracias. Thank you, Minister Arroyo. Minister Sturge of Trinidad and Tobago, you have the floor. Thank you. Secretary Hegseth, General Donovan. Thank you. I would first like to bring greetings on behalf of the Prime Minister of Trinidad and Tobago, the Honorable Kamla Persad-Bissessar, and to thank you for the invitation you have extended to us to attend this counter-cartel conference. We appreciate the solemnity of the occasion and we are grateful for it. For many years, Trinidad and Tobago's geopolitical position was a blessing, as a gateway connecting South America to the Caribbean and beyond. But that blessing has become a burden, as in recent times we've found ourselves at the mercy of powerful drug cartels and criminal networks engaged in narcotrafficking, firearms trafficking, and human trafficking. Trinidad and Tobago stands with the United States and all partners in confronting these cartels and transnational criminal organizations in our neck of the woods. We are not observers in this fight; we are on the front line with you. We have lost law enforcement officers in this fight, including one of our sailors during the interception of a semi-submersible that was transporting a couple tons of cocaine. We have lost thousands of citizens over the last two decades to violent crime, fueled mainly by the actions of the new narcoterroist networks. But our economic fortunes have changed, subjecting us to severe structural constraints which hinder us from delivering the appropriate level of border security needed. Because we were once classified as high-income earning and because of our GDP, that classification turns out to now be strategically misleading and thus limits our ability to access critical security assistance, donations of equipment, and other resources. This is at a time when our borders are critically exposed, leaving us at a critical disadvantage as we face off with sophisticated and well-financed narcotraffickers. They move their goods northwards into your neighborhoods, and onto your streets, into your homes and bringing destruction to your youth. If we are to deliver effectively as the security anchor in the southern Caribbean, we require assets that would equip us with the capability to disrupt the cartels in the transshipment corridors and to protect our shared economic interests in the energy sector. Every shipment intercepted in our waters is one that would never reach your streets. Again, permit me to underscore our commitment that we stand ready, our personnel are highly trained and our doctrine aligns with yours. We require assets in the interim that would enhance our maritime domain awareness as well as enable us to carry out targeted reinforcement that would serve both our interests and would deliver immediate hemispheric benefits. Our government is dedicated and ready to shoulder our responsibility. And once proportionately equipped to the expectations placed upon us, we will deliver results. I thank you. Thank you, Minister Sturge. At this time, Secretary Hegseth invites all heads of delegation to join him at the table for the signing of the Joint Security Declaration. It's the Americas Counter-Cartel Conference Joint Security Declaration, affirming our relationships, respecting sovereignty, recognizing the strategic importance of this hemisphere, and declaring our intent to expand multilateral and bilateral cooperation to enhance security in the hemisphere. We will cooperate on whole-of-government efforts on border security, countering narcoterroism and trafficking, securing critical infrastructure, and other areas to be determined. We are advancing peace through strength, as was so eloquently said by many speakers here. It means something to address future threats to our mutual interest and join together to combat narcoterroism and other shared threats in the Western Hemisphere. Signed March 5th, the year of our Lord 2026, in English and Spanish. How about that? Thank you. I'm the only one who needed to sign because everyone else already has. So here you have it. TL;DR Under "Operation Epic Fury," U.S. forces are systematically dismantling Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base and air defenses, highlighted by the recent destruction of a large Iranian drone carrier and the integration of a historic regional defense network. ...

TL;DR

Under "Operation Epic Fury," U.S. forces are systematically dismantling Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base and air defenses, highlighted by the recent destruction of a large Iranian drone carrier and the integration of a historic regional defense network.

Military Objectives and Tactical Progress

  • Strategic Destruction: The U.S. has transitioned to a phase of "razing" Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base. The objective is not just to neutralize current inventory but to destroy Iran's long-term ability to rebuild its arsenal.
  • Major Assets Neutralized:
      • Drone Carrier: U.S. forces recently struck an Iranian drone carrier ship (comparable in size to a WWII aircraft carrier), which remains on fire.
      • Air Defenses: Combined U.S. and Israeli combat power has "relentlessly destroyed" Iranian air defenses over the past several days.
  • Cost-Curve Innovation:
      • Interceptor Efficiency: The Pentagon has inverted the cost curve, now utilizing $10,000 weapons to down $100,000 Iranian drones, moving away from more expensive traditional missiles.
      • "Lucas" Drones: Adm. Cooper confirmed the use of "Lucas" drones—re-engineered captured Iranian technology now being deployed against Iranian targets.

Counter-Propaganda and Nuclear Policy

  • Debunking Disinformation: Secretary Hegseth dismissed Iranian claims of 500 U.S. casualties and the downing of an F-15 as "lies and deception" intended for domestic Iranian consumption.
  • Nuclear Stance: While specific operational plans for sites like Isfahan remain classified, Hegseth reiterated President Trump’s directive that Iran will never be permitted to possess a nuclear weapon.
  • Operational Security: The Department of War declined to confirm if special forces would be used to secure enriched uranium, stating that targets are struck based on specific desired outcomes.

Regional Coalition and Global Readiness

  • Allied Unity: Despite initial friction regarding the UK’s stance on Diego Garcia, Hegseth noted that basing issues are resolved and "bomber pulses" will increase in frequency.
  • "The 12-Country Factor": Adm. Cooper noted that Iran has now attacked 12 different countries. These actions have backfired, driving regional partners—including Saudi Arabia, UAE, Qatar, Bahrain, and Kuwait—closer into the "American orbit" for integrated defense.
  • Global Capacity: Hegseth emphasized that massing combat power in CENTCOM (Middle East) has not compromised other theaters:
      • SOUTHCOM: Ongoing counter-cartel operations in Central and South America.
      • Homeland: Continued focus on "locking down" the U.S. border.
      • INDOPACOM: Coordinated recent sinking of an Iranian ship in the Indian Ocean.

Guidance for the Iranian Public

  • Stay Home: The administration’s message to Iranian civilians is to "stay in your homes" and avoid protests while active bombing continues in Tehran and surrounding areas.
  • Targeting the Oppressors: Military efforts are specifically targeting IRGC and Basij units that have historically suppressed protesters, with the goal of eroding the regime's internal control.
  • Refugee Policy: Secretary Hegseth explicitly stated there is "no plan" for a wave of Middle Eastern refugees to be admitted to the U.S. as a result of the conflict, suggesting regional neighbors should provide any necessary support.
"We're not just hitting what they have, we're destroying their ability to rebuild... we will systematically dismantle Iran's missile production capability for the future."

— Adm. Brad Cooper

— Secretary Pete Hegseth

— Secretary Pete Hegseth

President Trump Participates in the Visit of the 2025 Major League Soccer Champions - Inter Miami CF

The White House(3.0M) ·32:03 ·58.1K views · 2026-03-05
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump hosted the 2025 MLS Cup Champions, Inter Miami CF, at the White House to celebrate their historic season and the impact of Lionel Messi on American soccer.
TL;DR President Trump hosted the 2025 MLS Cup Champions, Inter Miami CF, at the White House to celebrate their historic season and the impact of Lionel Messi on American soccer. During the event, the President also provided significant updates on military operations in Iran,...

TL;DR

President Trump hosted the 2025 MLS Cup Champions, Inter Miami CF, at the White House to celebrate their historic season and the impact of Lionel Messi on American soccer. During the event, the President also provided significant updates on military operations in Iran, energy security measures in the Gulf, and diplomatic progress in Venezuela and Cuba.

National Security and Middle East Operations

  • Military Action in Iran: President Trump reported that U.S. and Israeli forces are "totally demolishing" Iranian military capabilities ahead of schedule.
      • Strategic Impact: The U.S. claims to have destroyed approximately 60% of Iran's missiles and 64% of their launchers, alongside the destruction of 24 naval ships in three days.
      • Defection Offer: The administration is calling for the Iranian Revolutionary Guard and police to lay down their arms in exchange for total immunity, warning of "guaranteed death" for those who continue to fight.
  • Energy Security: To stabilize global markets, the administration has implemented political risk insurance for oil tankers transiting the Gulf.
      • Market Stability: The President attributed the Dow hitting 50,000 and the stabilization of oil prices to these decisive regional actions.

Diplomatic and Economic Updates

  • Venezuela Stabilization: Trump highlighted a working relationship with Venezuelan President-elect Delcy Rodriguez.
      • Oil Trade: Hundreds of millions of barrels of Venezuelan oil are being sent to Houston for refining, with the administration returning record-level payments to support the country's transition.
  • Cuba Policy: The President expressed optimism about future changes in Cuba, suggesting that Cuban exiles and business leaders like Jorge Mas would soon be able to return to a "free" country.
  • Trade and Tariffs: The President credited tariffs and aggressive deal-making for the current economic climate, noting that foreign leaders now describe the U.S. as the "hottest country in the world."

Inter Miami CF Championship Recognition

  • Lionel Messi’s Impact: Trump lauded Messi as a "tremendous champion," specifically noting that unlike other international stars who move to the U.S., Messi successfully delivered a championship under immense pressure.
      • Career Milestones: Recognition of Messi’s 47th career trophy and his status as the first player to be named MLS MVP twice in a row.
  • Historical Performance: The 2025 season was described as "record-shattering," with the team scoring 101 goals—the most in MLS history.
      • Global Competition: Inter Miami became the first North American club to defeat a European club in official competition after beating FC Porto in the FIFA Club World Cup.
  • Organizational Leadership: Praise was directed at owners Jorge and Jose Mas for their "winning culture" and Coach Javier Mascherano for winning the MLS Cup in his first year.

The Growth of American Soccer

  • Expansion of the Sport: Trump compared the current soccer boom to the era of Pelé and the New York Cosmos, noting that the sport has finally "caught on" in the U.S.
  • Upcoming Major Events: The President highlighted the 2026 FIFA World Cup and the upcoming Olympics as pivotal moments for the country’s sports legacy, noting record-breaking ticket sales for the World Cup.
  • The "Freedom to Dream": Owner Jorge Mas explained the team’s "Freedom to Dream" motto as a tribute to those who fled communism and tyranny to find success in the United States.

Pentagon official Elbridge Colby testifies on U.S. defense strategy amid Iran war — 3/5/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·164:26 ·15.5K views · 2026-03-05
Elbridge Colby (Under Secretary of Defense for Policy)
Under Secretary Elbridge Colby defended the 2026 National Defense Strategy’s "America First" shift toward Indo-Pacific deterrence and burden-sharing, while facing bipartisan condemnation for failing to consult Congress on troop withdrawals in Europe ...
TL;DR Under Secretary Elbridge Colby defended the 2026 National Defense Strategy’s "America First" shift toward Indo-Pacific deterrence and burden-sharing, while facing bipartisan condemnation for failing to consult Congress on troop withdrawals in Europe and the sudden...

TL;DR

Under Secretary Elbridge Colby defended the 2026 National Defense Strategy’s "America First" shift toward Indo-Pacific deterrence and burden-sharing, while facing bipartisan condemnation for failing to consult Congress on troop withdrawals in Europe and the sudden escalation of war with Iran (Operation Epic Fury).

2026 National Defense Strategy: Core Pillars

  • Primary Focus: The NDS prioritizes "flexible realism," focusing military resources on the U.S. homeland and the Indo-Pacific to prevent Chinese hegemony.
  • Denial Defense: Under Secretary Colby emphasized that the "primary focus" of U.S. Armed Forces is maintaining a denial defense along the first island chain to deter Chinese aggression.
  • NATO 3.0 and Burden Sharing: A central pillar of the strategy requires "wealthy allies" to assume primary responsibility for their own conventional defense.
      • Spending Targets: The administration expects NATO allies to reach 5% GDP spending by 2035.
      • Regional Leads: The U.S. is urging South Korea and European nations to take the lead in their respective theaters to allow the U.S. to "pace" against China.
  • Industrial Mobilization: The strategy calls for a "national mobilization" of the defense industrial base, supported by a proposed $1.5 trillion budget for FY 2027 to "supercharge" the U.S. arsenal.

Middle East Conflict and Operation Epic Fury

  • Operation Epic Fury: Launched on February 28, 2026, this military campaign seeks to degrade Iran’s missile production, one-way attack drone capabilities, and naval forces.
  • Strategic Contradictions: Ranking Member Adam Smith and Rep. Courtney challenged the administration on why the NDS explicitly downgraded the Iran threat just weeks before launching a war.
      • Objective: Colby defined success as the permanent degradation of Iranian power projection to prevent them from building a "conventional shield" for nuclear ambitions.
      • Cost of Conflict: Critics highlighted the loss of six U.S. service members and expressed concern that a prolonged war with Iran would deplete munitions needed for China deterrence.
  • The "Venezuela Model": Colby suggested the administration is looking for a political outcome in Iran similar to recent operations in Venezuela, hoping for the Iranian people to "rise up."

European Posture and Oversight Friction

  • Romania Withdrawal: Chairman Rogers (HASC) sharply criticized the decision to withdraw a U.S. brigade from Romania without consulting Congress or NATO allies, calling the move "incoherent" and a "deterrence gap" Russia will exploit.
  • Communication Breakdown: Members of both parties accused Colby of being "disingenuous" and "dishonest" regarding force posture changes.
      • Poland Commitment: Despite withdrawals elsewhere, Colby asserted the administration remains aligned with maintaining armored brigades in Poland.
      • Legislative Retaliation: Chairman Rogers warned that the committee may resort to legislation to force the "Department of War" to comply with constitutional oversight requirements.
  • Rebranding Controversy: Ranking Member Smith and others questioned the legal authority of the administration's shift in terminology from the "Department of Defense" to the "Department of War."

Defense Industrial Base and Readiness

  • Munitions Stockpiles: Rep. Stefanik and others pressed for specifics on how the $1.5 trillion budget would address critical shortages in the wake of active operations in the Middle East and the Caribbean.
  • Taiwan Support: Committee members expressed frustration that despite $12 billion in authorized funding for Taiwan’s defense, the department could not provide a status report on whether any of those funds had been executed.
  • Strategic Risks: Rep. Luttrell raised concerns about the vulnerability of subsea cabling and the lack of a clear "Department of War" policy for protecting the $10 trillion in daily transactions flowing through those systems.

Notable Quotes

"The American military, while without peer, is not infinite in its application and resources... we cannot do everything everywhere all the time."

— Elbridge Colby, Under Secretary of Defense for Policy

"It's been like pulling teeth [to get information]... the Constitution requires you and your team to have forthright conversations with this committee about the decisions you're making."

— Rep. Mike Rogers, HASC Chairman

"The Trump administration has decided that an excessive reliance on violence, threats, coercion, and the use of the military isn't just their go-to option—it's pretty much all they've got."

— Rep. Adam Smith, Ranking Member

"Our service members and the American people deserve better. You sit here today unable to provide us with any clarity [on the war in Iran]."

— Rep. Maggie Goodlander

Committee will come to order. Today we are meeting to review and examine the National Defense Strategy. The NDS sets the policy direction for the department's force planning, resource allocation, and strategic decision making. Our witness is the chief architect of that strategy and plays a central role in its implementation. I think he's having trouble hearing, Mr. Chairman. Not sure if that's the microphone. Is the microphone? Okay. All right. To date, we have seen real progress on key pillars of the NDS, such as defending the homeland and American interests in the Western Hemisphere. The southern border is secure. Operation Southern Spear is dismantling narco-terrorist networks, and the historic military operation that brought Nicolas Maduro to justice sent a clear message that the United States will no longer tolerate national security threats in our own backyard. I was also encouraged to see the strategy identifying rebuilding the defense industrial base as a critical line of effort. This will be a central focus of the FY 27 NDAA because achieving peace through strength depends on reviving America's arsenal of freedom. You will also find you have a willing partner in this committee to deter Chinese aggression. The One Big Beautiful Bill's $12 billion investment to improve readiness of U.S. forces in the Indo-Pacific and strengthen Taiwan's defense is proof of that commitment. That said, the strategy de-prioritizes U.S. interests in the Middle East and delegates primary responsibility for addressing the Iran threat to regional allies and partners like Israel. This stands in direct contrast to President Trump's decision this weekend to launch Operation Epic Fury. That decision underscored the President's belief that preventing Iran from acquiring a nuclear weapon and expanding its missile arsenal is a core U.S. national interest worth prioritizing. The strategy also ignores the threat posed by a Russian victory in Ukraine to U.S. interests. Let me be clear, I agree that Europe should take the lead in Ukraine's defense, and I welcome the fact that it's finally stepping up. But the President's relentless drive for peace reflects his clear understanding that a durable end to the war will require U.S. strength and resolve. That reality was difficult to square with the munitions review initiated by your office last year, which led to a temporary pause in U.S. military assistance to Ukraine. Fortunately, the President found out. He immediately overturned that pause, pledged additional U.S. weapons, and launched the Pearl Initiative to sustain U.S. arms deliveries with NATO funding. By the way, Congress and Ukraine learned of your munitions review only after the fact and not from your team, but from others in the administration who had also been left in the dark and shared our frustration. But what concerns me the most about the NDS is while your strategy hands over responsibility for deterring Russia's conventional threat to Europe, your policies are weakening the very allies you expect to assume that burden. President Trump's success in pushing NATO allies to commit to spending 5% of GDP on defense by 2035 was historic. But Europe cannot magically turn that new spending into credible forces and capabilities overnight. And yet you were pressuring allies to meet a 2027 deadline even though the department still has not provided clear guidance on exactly what capabilities Europe is expected to assume and what critical but more limited U.S. support would look like. It's already March of 2026. This is a recipe to guarantee failure and create a deterrence gap which Russia will exploit. Moreover, last year your team tried to zero out security cooperation funding for frontline NATO members like the Baltic States. That was a head scratcher for me given those modest investments are among the most effective tools for training and equipping allies to assume greater responsibility. But the decision to withdraw a U.S. brigade from Romania most starkly reflects this incoherent approach. Just days prior to the announcement, the President said any troop movements from Europe would be, quote, "little." A brigade is not little, but you went ahead and did it anyway. Neither Romania nor other NATO allies were consulted in advance. To be clear, after-the-fact notification just before an announcement is not consultation. And while I strongly disagree with the decision itself, advance coordination could have at least allowed our allies to replace the departing brigade to help cover the deterrence gap. I understand the need for our allies to take on more of a burden of their defense, but this must be done in a coordinated and responsible manner. You also need to make these types of decisions in consultation with your primary oversight committee in Congress. That would be this committee. We made repeated requests to be consulted on any potential U.S. force posture changes. Despite those requests, last October, just two weeks before the decision to withdraw the U.S. brigade from Romania, you told me during a meeting in your office at the Pentagon that you were not aware of any ongoing discussions of that type. I'm concerned that you weren't being truthful with me. This breakdown in communication is why we included oversight provisions in last year's NDAA requiring advance consultation with Congress on U.S. force posture changes. We may not always agree on a policy decision, but the Constitution requires you and your team to have forthright conversations with this committee about the decisions that you're making and why you're making them. We shouldn't have to resort to legislation to force you to have these conversations with us. Many of us here support the President's agenda. All of us here want to strengthen the national security. We can't achieve those goals if we're left in the dark. Thank you for being here today. I look forward to your testimony. With that, I yield to my friend, the ranking member, for any opening statement he may have. thank, thank you, Mr. Chairman, and I welcome the witness. We had the opportunity to talk yesterday. I appreciate that opportunity and it's really important that you come before this committee. And I think the real challenge here is there is the National Defense Strategy that was put out, and then there's the reality of what's, of what's going on. And I think we need to understand how, how those two mesh and what really is the National Defense Strategy in terms of what we're implementing. And on a basic level, we're trying to pursue our interests. But to do that, you have to figure out what those interests are. And we talk some of that is really detailed around material interests. Historically, it's been food and energy, but now the world is a more complicated place, so access to rare earth minerals. How do we keep the peace? We can cut taxes and spend $1.5 trillion on the defense budget without ever contemplating how do we use those resources better?
