But the overall biggest problem is the Trump administration has decided that an excessive reliance on violence, threats, coercion, and the use of the military isn't just their go-to option. I thank the ranking member. I'd now like to introduce our witness, Mr. Elbridge Colby is the Undersecretary of Defense for Policy. Thank you for being here, Mr. Colby, you are recognized. Mr. Chairman, Ranking Member Smith, thank you for the opportunity to appear before the distinguished members of this committee to testify on the Department of War's 2026 National Defense Strategy. The animating purpose of this strategy was to put into reality the logic of President Trump's common sense America First peace through strength approach. At its heart, as detailed in the 2025 National Security Strategy issued by the White House, this approach is channeled through the principle of flexible realism. That is the effort to practically and realistically advance and protect the concrete interests of regular Americans. The core of a strategy, as the National Security Strategy eloquently describes, is to evaluate, sort and prioritize. Needless to say, this is of special importance in the realm of war and peace. The heavy responsibility of effectively doing so motivated our work at the Department to produce the 2026 NDS and we believe under Secretary Hegseth's leadership that we have done so. Under President Trump's leadership and with your support in the Congress, the American military is and will be the world's finest, an unmatched fighting force that has impressively demonstrated its prowess in recent months in Operations Midnight Hammer, Absolute Resolve, and now Epic Fury. At the same time, we recognize that as Secretary Hegseth has aptly put it, we cannot do everything everywhere all the time. This is of course a practical reality. The American military, while without peer, is not infinite in its application and resources. Potential opponents have been building their strength and capabilities and it is not fair, just or sustainable to ask the American people to bear burdens that are inequitably distributed with those of our allies and partners. As a result, we must prioritize and this strategy does so. It does not do so in a way that is pinched or hesitant, instead it does so in a way that prudently and practically navigates between two unreasonable extremes. On the one hand, an isolationism that pretends that we can retreat from the world and that a favorable stability will emerge. And on the other hand, an unfettered use of military force for overly expansive ends that would drain the American people's will and resources in unnecessary and overambitious conflict. This strategy rather prudently adopts a middle course that will actually put us on a far firmer, more successful and thus more lasting course than either of these extreme alternatives. In particular, the NDS does so we contend by intelligently and strategically allocating our resources and that of our incredible armed forces towards the priorities that most affect regular Americans and where our efforts are most material and necessary to achieve American interests in the world. This is a reflection of common sense America First thinking. As a deduction, the strategy is built on four pillars. First, it restores after decades of neglect by some administrations the military's role in securing our homeland and ensuring that our key interests in our native hemisphere are protected. This is critical for regular Americans because we must ensure that we are directly protected and secure in our own home area. While the military is only a part of this effort and often far from the leading element, it does play a critical role that will be enduring. Second, the strategy focuses our military effort on ensuring the preservation of a favorable balance of power in the Indo-Pacific, the world's largest market area. America's interests there are real and significant because of the scale of that market. At the same time, our interests are not limitless. We do not need to dominate that region, rather we only need to prevent that vast and dynamic area from being dominated itself so that we can trade and interact with it on fair and reasonable terms for regular Americans. As a result, our strategy there is to deter China through strength, not confrontation. We understand that China is a very powerful country that is undertaking an extraordinary military buildup. That is a simple fact. At the same time, we are clear that we do not seek conflict with China. To the contrary, we seek to avoid it. We make clear that our interests with respect to China are scoped and reasonable. We do not seek to strangle China, nor compel a change in its form of government. Rather we seek to prevent China from becoming the hegemon of the Indo-Pacific. In particular, this requires as the National Security Strategy clearly states being able to deny the feasibility of successful aggression along the first island chain. Because of its significance and the central importance of the military role in meeting this requirement, meeting this standard of an effective denial defense along the first island chain is the primary focus of the U.S. Armed Forces. At the same time, ensuring our forces can pace to this challenging standard will ensure that the Department can always offer the President the operational flexibility and agility required for other objectives, especially the ability to launch decisive operations against targets anywhere, including as we see today in Operation Epic Fury. It is critical to emphasize here that our strategy is aligned not only with the interests of our allies in the Indo-Pacific, but with many other countries as well that are not formally our allies. These states seek to preserve their autonomy and thus their ability to chart their own national futures on their own terms. This too is our goal as Secretary Hegseth eloquently laid out last year at the Shangri-La Forum. And our military focus alongside the efforts of our allies and partners is designed to uphold that ability. Third, we recognize clearly that there are other real threats around the world that imperil our allies and indeed our own interests directly. We do not neglect that. To the contrary, we see it clearly. But we also understand that we need to allocate our own resources realistically and prudently and that we must account for our allies and partners' ability and will to meet those challenges as well. This is why the strategy so strongly emphasizes burden sharing, which is its third pillar. The logic here is to encourage, enable and wherever necessary to press our allies and partners in other theaters to take primary responsibility for their conventional defense. This makes sense because as is so often pointed out, we are allies with many of the world's largest and most advanced economies. As a result, we do indeed have an alliance network that has tremendous latent military power. Unfortunately over the last generation, partially due to our own permissiveness and perhaps even hubris on this point, many of our allies have functionally demilitarized. This is untenable and unreasonable and the fact is that our allies know it and are moving rapidly and substantially to step up. They are no longer because of President Trump's leadership fighting the scenario. Rather they have now accepted they must step up and are beginning rapidly to do so. As our result, our approach has adapted to one of let's get down to business. In Europe, we are urging our allies to move to a model of NATO 3.0 in which wealthy European allies take the lead for the conventional defense of European NATO. On the Korean Peninsula, we are urging our South Korean ally to do the same vis-a-vis North Korea. And the good news, distinguished members, is that this is working. Our allies are indeed stepping up along these lines. Now the issue is helping them do so and making this transition as rapidly and effectively as possible which is indeed our focus. In other regions, we are also urging and seeing progress with our allies. Along the first island chain with countries like Japan and Australia, we are urging these wealthy and capable allies to do their part for collective defense as the NSS urges. In the Western Hemisphere, we are working with many like-minded countries in the region to address shared threats to our prosperity and well-being as today's conference in Miami demonstrates. And in the Middle East, we are actively working with our model ally, Israel, and other regional partners to address the threat of Iran and terrorism, indeed the very purpose of Operation Epic Fury. The final pillar of the strategy as you mentioned, Mr. Chairman, is to supercharge the defense industrial base. The NSS and NDS, and I stress this, both call for a national mobilization of our industrial capacity to arm both our own forces and those of our allies and partners. As you on this committee know so well, this is vital because we must be able to arm our own forces and those of our allies and partners with the best weaponry, quickly, at scale and at a reasonable price. We are seeing tremendous progress on this front thanks to President Trump, Secretary Hegseth and Deputy Secretary Feinberg's efforts, as well as your support and leadership in Congress, but this will be a critical continuing area of focus in the coming years. In closing, this strategy promises under President Trump's leadership to put our country and our allies and partners on the path to lasting security. In just a few years, we can expect the warfighting ethos to have been restored in our armed forces and for them to be armed with the most modern effective weaponry at scale. We can expect our wealthy allies and partners around the world to have stepped up to do their part for collective defense and their own security. We can expect our potential opponents to see the peerlessly formidable armed forces of the United States and the highly capable forces of our allies and partners together and to conclude that peace and restraint is the better course for them. Thus we can very much reasonably hope for a decent peace for Americans, for our allies and for that matter for our potential opponents. This is a very good future to which we can reasonably aspire. At the same time, however, we at the Department of War know that we cannot take this future for granted. To the contrary, we will hope for the best but prepare for the worst. We will ensure that our armed forces are as ready as they can possibly be in line with the priorities outlined. Should our potential opponents spurn this reasonable offer, members of this committee, your support in ensuring that our forces are ready will be absolutely vital. The U.S. Is Falling Behind Its Global Peers When It Comes To Women In Leadership

Forbes(2.1M) ·18:39 ·443 views · 2026-03-05
Diana van Maasdijk (CEO, Equileap) Maggie McGrath (Editor, ForbesWomen)
Equileap’s eighth annual study of 3,500 global companies reveals that female leadership has reached a three-year plateau, with U.S.
TL;DR Equileap’s eighth annual study of 3,500 global companies reveals that female leadership has reached a three-year plateau, with U.S. corporations significantly regressing in transparency and representation compared to peers in Europe and Asia where government mandates are...

TL;DR

Equileap’s eighth annual study of 3,500 global companies reveals that female leadership has reached a three-year plateau, with U.S. corporations significantly regressing in transparency and representation compared to peers in Europe and Asia where government mandates are driving progress.

Global Stagnation in Leadership and Pay

  • Leadership Plateau: For the third consecutive year, the percentage of female CEOs globally remains stalled at just 7%.
      • Representation at the board and executive levels fluctuates narrowly between 20% and 30% with no significant upward movement.
  • The "Broken Pipeline": While women represent roughly 38–40% of the total workforce, representation drops significantly as seniority increases, confirming a persistent "leaking pipeline" in corporate hierarchies.
  • Board vs. Executive Disparity: Globally, women hold approximately 33% of board seats but only 7% of CEO positions.
      • Diana van Maasdijk notes that the 33% figure reflects years of targeted "30% club" initiatives, suggesting that progress only occurs when specific, publicized targets are set.

The U.S. Decline and Transparency Crisis

  • Falling Rankings: Only seven U.S. companies made the Global Top 100 list in 2024, a sharp decline from 17 companies just four years ago.
      • Top Performers: Despite the national decline, companies like International Flavors & Fragrances, Procter & Gamble, Boston Scientific, and Bank of America remain leaders in equity.
  • The Transparency Gap: The U.S. currently ranks last among developed markets for transparency regarding gender pay and representation data.
      • At the current rate of progress, it would take the U.S. nearly 40 years to reach the transparency standards that Europe achieved in just four years.
  • Institutional Regression: Equileap is observing a decline in U.S. companies publishing gender pay gaps and a reduction in supplier diversity programs, partly attributed to a shifting political climate surrounding Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (DEI).

Legislation as the Primary Catalyst

  • Mandated Progress: Research indicates that corporate gender balance rarely improves through "grassroots" or voluntary company efforts alone; it is almost exclusively driven by legislation.
  • Japan’s Rapid Shift: In 2021, 91% of Asia-Pacific companies did not disclose pay data. After Japan introduced mandatory disclosure laws, over 90% of Japanese companies now publish their gender pay gaps.
  • The French Model: After reaching 44% female representation on boards through quotas, France has now implemented new laws to force 30-40% representation at the executive committee level by 2030 because voluntary growth stalled.
  • Upcoming EU Directives: The EU Tax Transparency Directive is expected to further accelerate disclosure across Europe by mandating the publication of gender pay gaps for all large companies.

The Business Case and Performance Criteria

  • Financial Outperformance: Equileap’s data correlates higher gender equality scores with better financial performance. Indices tracking gender-equal companies consistently show higher revenue and profit compared to market averages.
  • Equileap’s Evaluation Metrics: Companies are scored across 19 criteria, including:
      • Four-Level Balance: Gender ratios at the Board, Executive, Senior Management, and Workforce levels.
      • Pay Equity: Publicly disclosing and actively closing the gender pay gap.
      • Parental Leave: Providing leave for both primary and secondary caregivers.
      • Policy Integrity: Anti-harassment and diversity policies within both the internal workforce and the supply chain.

Notable Quotes

"The truth is that I believe they [women] wouldn't be promoted at the same levels in Europe if there wasn't legislation that was forcing it... if you really just leave it to the companies and hope that there will be gender balance, you see that that movement does not happen." — Diana van Maasdijk
"According to your analysis, the U.S. ranks last among developed markets in transparency... it would take nearly four decades for the U.S. to reach the transparency standards that Europe achieved in four years." — Maggie McGrath
"Gender equality is not only a nice to have but it's a great to have when it comes to the financial benefit of investing in equality." — Diana van Maasdijk
Hi everyone, I'm Maggie McGrath, Editor of ForbesWomen. According to a new piece of concerning research, women's representation in corporate leadership is remaining stagnant on a global level. There are also concerning degrees of disclosure about equal pay and equal representation in levels of corporate governance. Joining us to discuss this new research is the CEO of the company that put it out. She is Diana van Maasdijk, CEO of Equileap. Diana, thank you so much for joining us. "Thank you Maggie, happy to be here." So Diana, every year for the past eight years, Equileap has analyzed some of the largest companies in the world. This data set includes nearly 3,500 companies across 24 markets. There are 10 billion dollars' worth of investment products. So you are looking at quite a large data set here. Can you take us through the top line findings when it comes to gender representation at some of these very large companies? "Yeah sure Maggie. So indeed, it's been the eighth year that we do this and we look at all the companies with a market cap of 2 billion US dollars or more that are listed in 24 economies around the world. So these are definitely the big names and the companies that you all recognize, that we all recognize. When it comes to findings, I would say there's three main findings. First one is that the number of women in leadership positions are just not moving. For the third year in a row, we see that we have 7 percent female CEOs. Only 7 percent. And we see that the percentage of the board and of the women in executives all go between 20, 30 percent and they really aren't moving that at all. If they're moving it's a one percentage here or there. So women in leadership is stagnating. Then the other finding that we also see is that there are some positive movements when it comes to the gender pay gap. There is much more transparency. Many more companies are publishing the gender pay gap. But why are they doing this? They're doing this because they're being forced to because of legislation in many countries, particularly in Europe, Australia, and Japan now has legislation to force companies to publish their pay gap. And we know that nothing changes if there's no transparency. So we know this is a good thing, but here again it's being forced. And finally in the largest economy in the world, in the United States, we're seeing regression when it comes to gender equality. We're seeing less companies publishing the gender pay gap, less companies having supplier diversity programs, and the number of women in leadership is here stagnating also." Your data is really interesting because if you look at it alongside other reports that come out year over year like the McKinsey Women in the Workplace report that they produce with LeanIn.org, this is very consistent. Your data is showing about 38, 40 percent representation of women just at all levels and then as you get to higher levels of seniority the representation of women goes down. I want to ask you about some of the companies that are doing it well. I have here the top 10 companies in the US. Only seven US companies made the top 100 in 2024. What does that say to you and what do you think is the reason for this decline? Is there a single culprit? "I think it's a number of things but indeed the decline is really clear. So this year only seven companies made it to the top 100 of the United States, seven US companies. The year before that it was 11, the year before that it was 15 and the year before that it was 17. So every year we're seeing less US companies coming into the top 100. I don't think we can pinpoint to one thing, but it has to do with what we are seeing right now: the importance that is being given from the government position, I think, towards what is gender equality and diversity. While in Europe there's legislation coming from governments to enforce transparency, to enforce quotas, what we're seeing in the United States is that there's definitely no legislation promoting this. At the same time we do know that right now there's a government in the United States that does not see diversity and equality as an important issue. So unfortunately this translates very clearly in how many companies are being transparent and how many companies are actually promoting gender equality." I would call out just to support what you just said, the US ranks last among developed markets in transparency and at the rate that you've analyzed it would take the US to reach transparency standards that Europe achieved in four years. And the truth is that I believe they wouldn't be promoted at the same levels in Europe if there wasn't legislation that was forcing it. So I do think that even though we now have a government that is against DEI there is a trend that was already happening. If you just leave it to the companies, if you really just leave it to the companies and hope that there will be gender balance, you see that that movement does not happen. A very clear example is France. France had a quota for women on boards as of 2011 I think, 2011 or 2012. Another piece that's really interesting to me here is the dichotomy between executive leadership and CEO leadership versus board director levels. Is this because there's one CEO versus a board of directors can be 10, 12 etc. people? So we do indeed have reached now globally 33 percent women on boards and in the United States that's 32 percent for the publicly listed companies. But again here the target was 30 percent and that's what we've gotten. It is still not parity because we should be seeing anything between 40 and 60 percent if we want to be talking about parity. So I think that when people want something and when they definitely make it very specific and they put targets, that's when we get there." I want to move to the transparency aspect of this report. "Legislation again. A very specific country, Japan, had the lowest transparency on gender equality for many years in our rankings. I guess my follow-up to that is: is it a bad thing that the laws are doing that? You've alluded a few times that it's legislation, legislation, legislation. Does that put it at risk for rollback and is it better when these sorts of initiatives come from a grassroots ground-up effect or could long-term gains be achieved because of legislation? "I think it would be better if it would come up from the companies themselves, if they would do this because they saw the benefit—the benefit of gender balance, the benefit when you have more equality within a corporation, the benefit when you are finding a talent pool from two different groups, from both men and women, not just within the men. I think that would be great if it would be that, but the benefit of having gender equality—if it goes faster then I think, and if we are realizing that it really is happening when someone puts a law, then I think that that maybe even though it's not as nice to have, it is something beneficial. In the end you do want corporations to get the best talent from both the female and the male pools and so I think that that's really important to then put the legislation in place so that that happens quicker." When we talk about the benefits of equal pay, equal representation, and diversity, equity and inclusion, I want to make it clear that we're not just talking about diversity for diversity's sake. There are legitimate business advantages to be gained from having more women on boards and in executive leadership. We at Forbes have found correlations between higher revenue and higher profit with the companies that have better balance on those positions of power. But I'm wondering Diana, in your research and analysis, what have you found that ties to—maybe it's a data point, an analysis point—what supports the case for gender parity and diversity in corporate leadership? "Yeah like you say Maggie, we have also found the same correlations. We have built indices together with index makers and whether they looked at the US market, at the French market, or at the emerging markets, they all always show us an outperformance when you choose the companies that have higher gender equality. So we know that gender equality is not only a 'nice to have' but it's a 'great to have' when it comes to the financial benefit of investing in equality." Is there anything else that stands out in this report? We've talked a lot about the US. What stands out to you about Europe or other regions that you have analyzed here? "Well, what stands out about Europe is definitely that there's the EU Tax Transparency Directive coming up this year and that's going to enforce all companies to publish their gender pay gap. So what we're seeing more at the European level is that the EU has pretty much stopped waiting and that they're now putting these legislations in place. What I also think that stands out from this report is that the percentage to make it to the top 100 has increased. This shows you that the top 100 companies are really doing a great job and those companies you mentioned in the United States, they're doing really a great job on gender equality. So we're really happy to be able to show that every year it's getting more and more difficult to get to the top 100. The companies which stay with the same score as last year end up having a lower place in the ranking, so you really have to continue to work hard at gender equality in order to make it to the top 100." That's really interesting, so the best are getting better. So I'll dig into some of the data. Procter & Gamble, which folks may know because of Puffs tissues or Bounty paper towels, they have a gender equity score of 75 percent. And that year-over-year change, you have a little red arrow down, so that means they had a higher gender equity score last year but they're still in the top 10. What does it take to achieve a gender equity score of 75 percent? Is that the composition of the board plus executive management? "Well we look at 19 criteria, different criteria. So we're looking at the gender balance at four levels within the company: at the board, at the executive level, at the senior management, and at the workforce. So if you have anything between 40 and 60 percent you get the highest numbers. So it means having some form of gender balance at these four levels. It also means: what are you doing for the gender pay gap? Are you publishing it? Are you trying to close it? Do you have any strategies to address the gender pay gap? Do you give parental leave to both primary and secondary caregivers and do you have policies in place both in your supply chain and in your workforce to promote diversity and equality? Now, so if you have all of these things in a little bit of everything, that's when you make it. The average score this year is around 44 percent, so then you can see that someone who has 75 percent is definitely going to be in the top 100." What do you think the scores will do over the course of this year? You've been doing this research for eight years now or publishing it for eight years. If you had to guess, will we see a collective drop between 2024 and 2025 given what we've seen in certain state and country governments? Will we see that given what we've seen with the trends overall, or is there hope that the declines and stagnation that you talked about at the beginning will start to reverse? "I think we will continue to see the same trend with Europe and Asia continuing to improve, many times because of legislation. The United States, which makes up half of the universe of companies that we research for this report, the United States will continue to decline. And so that is unfortunate. When you balance that out, we will see that we continue to see this stagnation." Is there any call to action you would give to the Forbes audience which includes entrepreneurs, CEOs, and aspiring entrepreneurs, but a number of people who do have hiring power? What would you like them to know and or do in the coming year? "Yeah sure. I mean you have hiring power, you have buying power, and you have investment power as a consumer. So I would say speak to your investment manager and say, 'Are we investing in the companies that have more gender equality?' because that is available. There are investment funds in the United States where you can invest in the companies that have more gender equality; you can ask your investors. You can also look at the top 100 and decide who you want to buy from. And when you are working for the different companies, you can talk to your colleagues and be like, 'Do we have targets in place, are we ensuring that we find the right people, both who are men and women, to bring to our teams?'" Diana van Maasdijk, thank you so much for joining us. This is such important research. We appreciate you putting the time to bring it to the world and thank you for talking to us about it today. "Thank you for inviting me."

Live Iran War Update: Pete Hegseth on Operation Epic Fury

FOX 5 Washington DC(638.0K) ·33:15 ·2.5K views · 2026-03-05
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of War)
Operation Epic Fury has achieved total air dominance and rendered the Iranian Navy combat ineffective within six days, utilizing "maximum authorities" to systematically dismantle Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base and neutralize its power projection.
TL;DR Operation Epic Fury has achieved total air dominance and rendered the Iranian Navy combat ineffective within six days, utilizing "maximum authorities" to systematically dismantle Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base and neutralize its power projection. Naval...

TL;DR

Operation Epic Fury has achieved total air dominance and rendered the Iranian Navy combat ineffective within six days, utilizing "maximum authorities" to systematically dismantle Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base and neutralize its power projection.

Current Combat Status and Key Metrics

  • Naval Degradation: U.S. forces have destroyed or sunk over 30 Iranian ships to date.
      • In the last few hours, a B-2 strike hit an Iranian drone carrier (roughly the size of a WWII aircraft carrier), which remains on fire.
  • Degraded Strike Capabilities: Iranian offensive capabilities have plummeted since the start of the operation.
      • Ballistic Missiles: Attacks have decreased by 90% since Day One.
      • Drones: Attacks have decreased by 83% since Day One.
  • Air Dominance: U.S. bombers have struck nearly 200 targets deep inside Iran (including Tehran) in the last 72 hours.
      • Recent B-2 bomber missions utilized 2,000-pound penetrator bombs to target deeply buried ballistic missile launchers.
  • Space Command: Strikes have successfully targeted and degraded Iran’s equivalent of Space Command to disrupt their tracking and targeting capabilities.

Strategic Objectives and Command Authority

  • Maximum Authority: Secretary Hegseth emphasized that unlike the "politically correct wars of the past," Admiral Cooper has been granted "maxed out" authorities and clear objectives to use overwhelming force.
  • Industrial Attrition: The mission has transitioned into a phase focused on "razing" Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base. The goal is not just to destroy current inventory but to eliminate the nation's ability to rebuild its arsenal.
  • Sustainability: The U.S. maintains full stockpiles of offensive and defensive munitions. Hegseth noted that the timeline is entirely under U.S. control, with combat power expected to "surge dramatically" as more basing and fighter squadrons become operational.
  • Nuclear Redline: Hegseth reaffirmed the administration's stance that Iran will never possess a nuclear weapon, though he declined to discuss specific operational plans regarding enriched uranium sites in Isfahan.

Regional Alliances and Technological Innovation

  • Coalition Unity: Despite the UK's initial hesitation regarding Diego Garcia, the U.S. has secured necessary basing. Iran’s attacks on 12 different countries have "simplified" the mission by pulling regional partners (UAE, Qatar, Bahrain, Saudi Arabia, Kuwait) into a tighter "American orbit."
  • The "Cost Curve" Shift: Admiral Cooper highlighted a shift in the economics of drone warfare. The U.S. is now successfully intercepting $100,000 Iranian drones with $10,000 U.S. weapons.
  • LUKAS Drones: The U.S. is successfully deploying the "LUKAS" drone—a captured and re-engineered Iranian design—against Iranian targets.
  • IDF Coordination: U.S. forces are operating "shoulder-to-shoulder" with the Israeli Defense Forces, creating the most integrated air defense network in the history of the Middle East.

Counter-Propaganda and Domestic Security

  • Casualty Verification: Hegseth formally debunked Iranian propaganda claiming 500 American deaths and the shoot-down of an F-15 in Iraq, labeling these reports as "lies" intended for Iranian domestic consumption.
  • Honoring the Fallen: The U.S. is preparing to receive six fallen service members at Dover Air Force Base who were killed during this mission.
  • Refugee Policy: Hegseth explicitly stated there is "no plan" to accept a wave of Middle Eastern refugees into the U.S. as a result of this conflict, suggesting regional partners are better equipped for such support.
  • Homeland Security: Regarding the transition at DHS from Kristi Noem to Markwayne Mullin, Hegseth expressed total confidence that homeland security and border lockdown efforts will remain uninterrupted.
"The dumb, politically correct wars of the past were the opposite of what we’re doing here. They had vague objectives with restrictive, minimalist rules of engagement. No more. Our authorities... are maxed out." — Pete Hegseth, Secretary of War

TL;DR

Operation Epic Fury has entered its sixth day, successfully neutralizing Iran’s naval combat effectiveness and reducing ballistic missile attacks by 90%. The mission is now shifting toward the systematic destruction of Iran’s industrial base to prevent rearmament, supported by "maxed out" military authorities and a surge of incoming combat power.

Current Operational Impact and Metrics

  • Degradation of Iranian Assets: Admiral Brad Cooper reported significant losses to Iranian offensive capabilities within the first six days of the operation.
      • Naval Losses: Over 30 Iranian ships have been sunk or destroyed, including a drone carrier roughly the size of a WWII aircraft carrier.
      • Missile and Drone Reduction: Ballistic missile attacks have decreased by 90% and drone attacks by 83% since the commencement of the operation.
      • Targeting Depth: In the last 72 hours, U.S. bombers struck nearly 200 targets deep inside Iran, including sites near Tehran and "Space Command" facilities.
  • Air Dominance: U.S. forces have established total dominance over Iranian airspace.
      • B-2 Operations: Within the last hour of the briefing, B-2 bombers utilized 2,000-pound penetrator bombs to strike deeply buried ballistic missile launchers.
      • Integrated Defense: The U.S. and regional partners have established the most integrated air defense network in Middle East history.

Strategic Shift and Future Objectives

  • Dismantling Industrial Capacity: Secretary Pete Hegseth emphasized that the mission is transitioning from hitting existing stockpiles to "razing" Iran’s ballistic missile industrial base.
      • Rebuild Prevention: The objective is to ensure Iran lacks the physical infrastructure to manufacture or replace destroyed weaponry in the future.
  • Maximum Battlefield Authorities: Hegseth distinguished this campaign from "politically correct wars of the past," stating that commanders now have "maxed out" authorities and clear objectives without restrictive rules of engagement.
  • Surging Combat Power: Leadership indicated that current operations represent only the beginning.
      • Force Flow: Significant additional fighter squadrons and bomber pulses are scheduled to arrive, intended to multiply the current level of projected power over Tehran.
      • Munitions Sustainability: Hegseth assured that U.S. munitions stockpiles are "full up" and capable of sustaining a long-term campaign.

Regional Diplomacy and Intelligence

  • Coalition Unity: Contrary to reports of regional chaos, Admiral Cooper noted that Iran has attacked 12 different countries, inadvertently driving nations like Saudi Arabia, UAE, Qatar, and Kuwait into a firmer "American orbit" for basing and defensive cooperation.
  • Counter-Propaganda: Secretary Hegseth addressed Iranian "lies," specifically debunking claims that an American F-15 was shot down or that 500 Americans had been killed.
      • Information Control: Leadership noted the Iranian regime has shut down the internet to propagandize its own population.
  • Technological Innovation: The U.S. is utilizing "Lucas" drones—re-engineered versions of captured Iranian designs—as a low-cost alternative for striking Iranian targets, effectively "getting on the other side of the cost curve."

Domestic and Humanitarian Considerations

  • Casualties: The U.S. is preparing to receive six fallen service members at Dover Air Force Base; Hegseth framed their sacrifice as a catalyst for rededicating the mission.
  • Civilian Protection: Residents of Iran have been advised to "lay low" and stay in their homes to avoid being caught in strikes targeting IRGC headquarters often located near residential areas.
  • Refugee Policy: Hegseth stated there is currently "no plan" for a wave of Middle Eastern refugees to enter the United States, suggesting regional neighbors are better equipped to provide such support.
  • Homeland Security: Despite leadership changes at the Department of Homeland Security, the Secretary expressed total confidence in the continued security of the U.S. border and homeland.

FULL EVENT: The 2026 Americas Counter Cartel Conference including Pete Hegseth - 03/05/26

Right Side Broadcasting Network(2.1M) ·106:14 ·9.5K views · 2026-03-05
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of War)
The 2026 Americas Counter-Cartel Conference marks a fundamental strategic shift in U.S.
TL;DR The 2026 Americas Counter-Cartel Conference marks a fundamental strategic shift in U.S. policy, reclassifying drug cartels as "narco-terrorists" and treating the Western Hemisphere as a primary theater of U.S. Homeland Defense under a revived Monroe Doctrine. The...

TL;DR

The 2026 Americas Counter-Cartel Conference marks a fundamental strategic shift in U.S. policy, reclassifying drug cartels as "narco-terrorists" and treating the Western Hemisphere as a primary theater of U.S. Homeland Defense under a revived Monroe Doctrine. The administration is pivoting from a law enforcement approach to a military-led strategy of "lethal force" and "peace through strength" to dismantle criminal networks.

Strategic Shift: From Criminal Justice to Military Force

  • Designation of Narco-Terrorism: Secretary of War Pete Hegseth and other leaders explicitly compared cartels to ISIS and Al-Qaeda, arguing they cannot be defeated through traditional law enforcement or legal frameworks alone.
  • The "War Department" Framework: Under the 2026 National Defense Strategy, the Americas are prioritized as a direct extension of U.S. homeland defense.
      • Lethal Force: Hegseth emphasized that the U.S. is prepared to use "hard power" and the "tip of a very lethal spear" to eradicate cartels.
      • Rejecting "Soft" Advice: Hegseth critiqued decades of "bad advice" from NGOs that advocated for demilitarization and softer criminal justice approaches, calling instead for the establishment of "order first" through ruthless means if necessary.
  • Restoring Deterrence: Joseph Humire (Acting Assistant Secretary of War) distinguished between "interdiction" (seizing drugs) and "deterrence" (offensive actions that destroy the threat). He noted that the goal is to make cartel activity "suicidal" for the organizations involved.

Hemispheric Defense and the Monroe Doctrine

  • Regional Sovereignty: The administration emphasized that "territorial integrity" is the bedrock of democracy. Any area controlled by a cartel is viewed as a failure of sovereignty.
  • Monroe Doctrine Legacy: Humire explicitly linked current policy to the 1823 Monroe Doctrine, suggesting that the U.S. and its neighbors must unite to keep the hemisphere free from "malign agendas" and foreign-influenced criminal entities.
  • The Migration-Crime Nexus: Speakers linked illegal mass migration directly to cartel enrichment. By securing borders and defeating cartels, the administration aims to stop the "human capital flight" that impoverishes partner nations while funding criminal assassinations and violence.

Operational Initiatives and "Operation Southern Spear"

  • Active Operations: General Francis Donovan highlighted Operation Southern Spear and Operation Absolute Resolve as current evidence of the U.S. commitment to restoring deterrence through action rather than dialogue.
  • Collective Security: The conference focused on moving beyond "lip service" to actual burden-sharing, including:
      • Intelligence Sharing: Establishing a "hub" for real-time data to track maritime, aerial, and cyber threats.
      • Interoperability: Aligning military equipment and training (e.g., U.S. Navy SEALs training El Salvador’s "Trident" forces).
      • Maritime Surveillance: Costa Rica and the Bahamas emphasized the importance of "joint patrolling" treaties that allow U.S. vessels to exercise jurisdictional authority in partner waters to intercept shipments.

Partner Nation Commitments and Models

  • The "El Salvador Model": The Salvadoran representative received significant attention for describing the country’s transition from the "homicide capital of the world" to the safest in the hemisphere by using the military to crush gangs and ignoring international NGO criticism.
  • Strategic Modernization:
      • Guatemala: Implementing Operation Cinturón de Fuego (Belt of Fire) to seal its porous border with Mexico.
      • Ecuador: Moving from a "transit country" to a nation in "internal armed conflict," using the military to restore state authority over ports.
      • Paraguay: Highlighting the SOFA (Status of Forces Agreement) with the U.S. to restart bilateral military cooperation.
      • Jamaica: Credited U.S. support and the "Secure Jamaica" plan for a 40% reduction in murders over the past year.

***

Notable Quotes

"The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations."

— Pete Hegseth, Secretary of War

"If you do not control your borders, you do not have a country. . . You do not combat transnational crime with transnational institutions; you fight it with borders and national sovereignty."

— Joseph Humire, Acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense

"The person who perdones the wolf sacrifices the sheep. . . The state cannot be weaker than the crime."

— Representative of El Salvador (referencing President Nayib Bukele)

"The Western Hemisphere is our neighborhood, and the narco-terrorists who seek to export drugs and death to our shores and your shores will be dismantled and defeated."

— General Francis Donovan, Commander of U.S. Southern Command

Narco-traffickers and drug cartels engage in campaigns of terror, violence, and corruption to enrich themselves and expand their insidious influence. They are poisoning and killing all of our nation's citizens. These sophisticated networks flood our streets with fentanyl, cocaine, and other drugs that kill at a higher rate than any conventional war. This is the single gravest threat to security in our region and across our hemisphere. To counter this threat, the United States is guided by the strategic vision of peace through security, set forth in both the United States National Security Strategy and the National Defense Strategy. These documents give our military a clear mandate in the Western Hemisphere to defend the US homeland and increase burden sharing with our allies and partners to do so. We are your premier partner to work with, by, with, and through your nations to achieve shared objectives. But when necessary, we will not hesitate to act. Operation Southern Spear and Absolute Resolve are proof of this commitment. Through these efforts, the United States is combating narco-terrorism and drug trafficking and, most importantly, restoring deterrence. The Western Hemisphere is our neighborhood, and the narco-terrorists who seek to export drugs and death to our shores and your shores will be dismantled and defeated. As allies and partners, we must take aggressive action together, sharing the burden through cooperation. We now call upon our longstanding relationships built on a foundation of trust and linked through shared culture, history, and economic aspirations to unite for the benefit of all of our nations. Our mutual security is at stake. Through combined operations, training exercises, and intelligence and information sharing, we will achieve greater domain awareness, interoperability, and capacity to strengthen regional security. With all nations shouldering this burden, our collective resilience will prevent shared adversaries from using the region to further their malign agendas. The time is now. Today marks the next step towards moving out to deter and defeat those who threaten the security, stability, and prosperity of the United States and this hemisphere. Let's get to work. Thank you, General Donovan. Ladies and gentlemen, performing the duties of Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and Americas Security Affairs, Joseph Humire. Good morning. Buenos dias. And I'd be remiss if I didn't begin by thanking my new best friend General Donovan for your excellent remarks and, frankly, for your excellent teamwork here at SOUTHCOM. Your team has truly been amazing at putting all this together in just a matter of a few weeks. So thank you, General. I'd also like to begin by acknowledging a special team of senior advisors and defense officials who have nurtured efforts, and not just today, but over the last several months. We frankly just wouldn't be here today. So a special thank you to Principal Deputy Assistant Secretary Divelbiss, Acting Deputy Assistant Secretary Strasba, Mr. Eric Garissi, Senior Advisor Mateo Haydar, and a special sincere and heartfelt thank you to Senior Advisors Philip Hegseth and Patrick Weaver. If it wasn't for you, frankly, this would not have happened. To all our interagency friends and foreign partners, this is truly a historic moment for the United States, for the Western Hemisphere, and for the world. In the room today, we have senior leadership from the Department of State, Department of Homeland Security, National Security Council, Homeland Security Council, Office of National Drug Control Policy, Office of the Vice President, and of course the White House. I'm honored to be here with you today as Secretary Hegseth's acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas. Welcome everyone to the first Americas Counter-Cartel Conference in history. Every morning before I enter the Pentagon, I say a prayer in my car to our Heavenly Father. And I do this because I'm blessed. I'm blessed to be able to work in a once-in-a-lifetime administration, to work for a once-in-a-generation leader, President Donald J. Trump. And frankly, if it wasn't for President Trump, none of us, including our foreign partners, would be here today. President Trump, Secretary Hegseth, Mr. Stephen Miller—our leadership today understands an empirical truth that all the leadership of the United States missed for decades, if not centuries. They missed the fact that the Americas is an extension of US homeland defense, and that hemispheric defense is a part of US homeland defense. This statement of fact is now reflected in US policy through the 2025 National Security Strategy and the 2026 National Defense Strategy that correctly places the Americas as a regional priority. Just think about where we were a little over one year ago today. Those of you that know me know that I've been working and studying on regional issues for more than 20 years. So I can confidently say that in 2024, prior to President Trump, I had never seen the Americas in worse shape. Prior to President Trump, there was record-breaking illegal mass migration that was collapsing our border and many of your borders as well. There was record-breaking deaths of Americans from deadly drugs that was transiting through your countries and stealing your sovereignty. The explosion of transnational organized crime in every single country throughout the Americas was destroying our shared region. If you think about it, before President Trump, we were breaking all the records for all the wrong things. And it was even worse because there was a growing false narrative throughout the region that was taking hold that said you cannot win a war against major cartels and gangs because they are too powerful. In some cases and in some countries, frankly, they were giving up and adopting a posture of "if you can't beat them, join them." That's not tolerable anymore. Not for the United States, not for your countries, and not for our neighborhood. If you look to your right and you look to your left, you will find partners that are willing to fight with you. And if we collectively fight together, there is no gang, there is no terrorist, there is no cartel that is more powerful than us. We are here today because each of you has committed to doing your part. And that commitment cannot just be lip service. This must come with a new approach and a new normal to dismantle and destroy our shared threats of cartels and narco-terrorism. There will be a lot of discussions today in a myriad of topics, but I really want to quickly emphasize two that are essential to this effort: border security and deterrence. If you do not control your borders, you do not have a country. Border security and territorial integrity, as President Trump has shown, is possible. Since 1648, since the Peace of Westphalia, sovereignty has been the bedrock of human flourishing. You do not have a democracy if you do not have your sovereignty, and you cannot have sovereignty without borders. Open borders make transnational organized crime fundamentally transnational. And you do not combat transnational crime with transnational institutions; you fight it with borders and national sovereignty. If we remove the transnational from transnational organized crime, it becomes a local problem, and it becomes a solvable problem. The second point is on deterrence. Interdiction while necessary is not deterrence. Dialogue while useful is not deterrence. Failing to control your prisons, your borders, or your waters is not deterrence. Going on offense is deterrence. Operation Southern Spear shows that deterrence is taking lethal and bold actions, and our goal is for every partner in this room today to start leading deterrence-focused offensive operations against your cartels. And finally, to wrap up, I want to make a point about geography. Geography in this hemisphere is indispensable to security. I mean the secret sauce of Latin America and the Caribbean to be a regional priority for the United States—it's not the size of your trade, it's not your populations or your military, it's because we are always going to be connected because of our shared geography. The United States understood this going back to 1815, when two leaders in a relatively young Department of War pushed back against their own establishment at that time, not to look north or to look east but rather to look south. The Department of War back then fought and won the Battle of New Orleans in 1815 that connected the United States to what is now the Gulf of America. And that moment 210 years ago connected my country to yours. And just a few years after that, these same two leaders led the Department of War to fight another war on our southern border, the Seminole Wars. This time it was to protect our borders against Indian tribes that trespassed our land, raided our towns, and killed Americans. We ultimately won these battles and transferred the great state of Florida in 1821. And in a mere two years later, one of these leaders declared something called the Monroe Doctrine. The legacy of then Secretary of War James Monroe, who later became President James Monroe, and Major General Andrew Jackson, who also later became President, defines the challenge that we have before us today. They say history doesn't repeat itself, but it tends to rhyme. And our interest over this shared geography is the same today as it was 200 years ago. Today, the Department of War once again has historic leadership under Secretary Pete Hegseth, committed to working with all of you to secure the Western Hemisphere once and for all. Thank you, God bless you. Thank you, General Donovan. Ladies and gentlemen, performing the duties of Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and Americas Security Affairs, Joseph Humire. Thank you General Nordhaus and thank you to my good friend Secretary Hegseth, who I've long admired but I've gotten to know personally in the last number of months. He is as fine a gentleman and as fierce a warrior as you will ever meet, and we've all learned to put our faith and confidence in his leadership of the War Department. So thank you, Pete. There's been a paradigm shift under President Trump in the Western Hemisphere in the last 14 months. Some of that was touched on by the earlier speakers but just to restate it, we are not going to cede an inch of territory in this hemisphere to our enemies or our adversaries. Our national security, our homeland security, the safety and well-being of our people begins at home, begins in our neighborhood, begins in our home region. And for too long we have allowed foreign enemies, foreign adversaries, and enemy terrorist organizations to control territory and spaces in this hemisphere where they can project power, project threats, and directly threaten the lives of your citizens and our citizens. And so under the leadership of President Trump, we are using hard power, military power, lethal force to protect and defend the American homeland. Not a single one of your nations should tolerate the existence of a single square mile of territory that is under the control of any entity other than the sovereign governments of your country. The idea that we would have in this hemisphere areas that are under the physical control of foreign terrorist organizations or paramilitary organizations is completely unacceptable. The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations. We have learned after decades of effort that there is not a criminal justice solution to the cartel problem. There are elements of the problem that require a criminal justice solution to be sure, but just as we fought Al-Qaeda and fought ISIS with the tip of a very lethal spear, the reason why this is a conference with military leadership and not a conference of lawyers is because these organizations can only be defeated with military power. And I see some heads nodding up front because they understand you're dealing with a lot of lawyers in your own country, I'm sure. You have my permission not to listen to them. The key condition of human flourishing, of prosperity, is order and security. A country cannot pursue economic growth, cultural enrichment, art, architecture, education, science, if it doesn't have fundamental security first. And for too long we've been caught in a vicious cycle in this hemisphere where countries from Mexico down to the tip of South America do not provide their citizens with basic physical security. So those citizens in search of economic opportunity illegally immigrate to the United States. They pay the smugglers, they pay the cartels, who then grow richer, who then grow more powerful, and then your countries in turn become poor because you've lost your citizens, you've lost your human capital, you've lost your opportunities to develop and to grow. So you get poor, the cartels get more powerful, nothing changes, nothing improves. You have to break that cycle. You have to completely sever it. And that begins by dismantling these organizations that deal drugs, deal violence, assassinate, extort, blackmail, and also traffic illegally in human beings. That is a form of terrorism too. Illegal immigration facilitated by criminal networks is a form of terrorism. And those who are stealing your children and stealing your families and stealing your human capital are part of that terrorist continuum and should be thought of as such. It is not a victimless crime when people are paying human smuggling organizations, because that money then fuels assassinations, it fuels violence, it fuels instability and it continues this cycle. For decades, a lot of organizations were sent to your countries, these are known as NGOs—I think you know the term non-governmental organizations—and they gave you very, very bad advice for a very, very long time. And basically they told you in short that you needed to be softer and gentler and nicer to the criminals and that you had to demilitarize your approach and extend more rights and more opportunities to the people who were sabotaging and destroying your society. I would make the point to you as others have pointed out before, including the President of El Salvador, that the West only achieved the place that it did in human history because it spent previous centuries eradicating the criminal elements within its territories. So the West that we came to know that had the great music and architecture and science and the most powerful economy, it had spent centuries previous to that establishing order first. So that by the time you get, you know, say 18th century England or 19th century England, there had been centuries of previous history in which ruthless means were used to get rid of the people who were raping and murdering and defying established systems of order and justice. And then when people could live freely and peaceably and children and families and women could be safe, then it became possible to pursue all of these higher elements of life. So when people are coming to countries like yours that have had struggles and difficulties and pains and challenges and they're telling you anything other than first and foremost secure your countries and your territory, they are giving you not only terrible advice but advice that if someone had come to Europe centuries ago and they had followed that advice, Europe would have never become the Europe that it eventually became. And the reason why many Western countries are struggling today is they've forgotten the eternal truths and wisdoms they once followed. And that's why here under President Trump in America we are returning to those fundamental truths, we're returning to those fundamental principles. So I'll just close by saying that you will have in President Trump, in the White House, in the Department of War, the Department of State and the whole of our government unwavering support, unwavering commitment and fidelity in achieving this mission, which is very simply to eradicate the criminal cartels and terrorist organizations to deprive them of control of weapons, spaces, institutions and territory. And the human rights that we're going to protect are not of the savages that torture and rape and murder; the human rights that we are going to protect are of the peaceable citizens who have an absolute human right to live in physical safety and security every day of their lives. And in that mission I promise you we will be your closest partner. Thank you. Thank you, Secretary Hegseth. We now invite each of our partners to provide short remarks. On behalf of the government and people of the Commonwealth of the Bahamas, I extend warm appreciation to Secretary Hegseth and the government of the United States for convening this conference and for the seriousness of purpose which it signals to criminal networks across this hemisphere. Let me be direct. This gathering is a declaration that we will no longer permit cartel networks, narcotics traffickers, and organized criminal enterprises to exploit the gaps between and within our jurisdictions. And the Bahamas stands fully committed to that declaration. Allow me to use this occasion to reiterate that the military cooperation between the Bahamas and the United States must be based on the shared commitment to peace, sovereignty, and security, consistent with our existing bilateral agreements and with full respect for sovereignty and laws of each country and with due regard to international legal norms. The Bahamas intends to work closely with the United States Department of War in expanding joint military cooperation, recognizing the shared responsibility for regional security and stability. The Bahamas welcomes closer collaboration on military-to-military efforts regarding border security, counter-narcotics, interoperability, and intelligence sharing. The disparity between the scale of our security responsibility and our resource capacity is significant and it is not sustainable. Our vast maritime domain requires continuous aerial and maritime patrols, our surveillance infrastructure requires sustained capital investment, and our security personnels require advanced training in counter-narcotics, counter-trafficking, and cyber defense. The concept of peace through strength carries enduring resonances, and if we are candid, enduring misapplications. For a small state like the Bahamas, it is not about arsenal size; it is about the integrity of our institutions, the credibility of our courts, and the depth of our partnerships. Hence peace through strength as we understand it is not unilateral; it is collective. It rests on the recognition that hemispheric security is indivisible, that what weakens Nassau ultimately weakens Miami, that instability in Port-au-Prince reaches Nassau long before it reaches Washington, and that small states on the frontline of transnational crime deserve genuine partners, not conditional patrons. Let me close by stating the Bahamas' commitment and expectation with the directness this forum warrants. The Bahamas will continue to operate as a frontline maritime security partner, disrupting narcotics trafficking routes, denying criminal networks the use of our waters and airspace, and contributing meaningfully to the security architecture of this hemisphere. The Bahamas brings to this partnership something no defense budget can manufacture: geography, institutional commitment, and a track record of cooperation spanning more than four decades. We ask only what we give: genuine partnership grounded in mutual respect and shared responsibility. The Americas either secures itself collectively or it fails collectively. The Commonwealth of the Bahamas has chosen collective security, and we invite every partner assembled here to meet that commitment with equal resolve, equal investment, and equal determination. Good morning everyone. I am here today representing the government of the Republic of Ecuador. I stand before this multilateral forum not only as a Minister of Defense but as the spokesman for a nation that has decided with absolute firmness and political will to confront the greatest threat of our contemporary history: transnational narco-terrorism. We are at a turning point. The decisions we make at this conference will directly impact the security and stability of our future generations. Historically, our nations have shared the values of democracy, freedom, and the rule of law. Today those values demand a common strategic response from us. On the basis of this principle, our national defense policy aligns with the United States National Defense Strategy. Ecuador recognizes that border security is an essential component of national and hemispheric security. The threats we face have mutated. We are not dealing with dispersed criminal networks but with destabilizing structures with transnational scope and links that erode democratic institutions. Narco-terrorist organizations not only traffic drugs, they challenge the legitimate authority of the state and affect the freedom and prosperity of our citizens. The security of our hemisphere is indivisible. Every nation that strengthens its capacities contributes to regional stability. In this context, Ecuador's commitment to its own security also constitutes an important contribution to the security of the Americas. During the last few years, Ecuador has ceased to be merely a transit country and has become one of the main departure points for drug trafficking to international markets. This evolution of criminal activity has generated a wave of unprecedented violence in our recent history. In addition to this, there exists a structural factor that cannot be ignored: the expansion of illicit crops in the region and the modernization of production and distribution chains. As long as these dynamics persist, the pressure on our territories will continue, confirming that we face a challenge that transcends borders and demands effective cooperation among states. To address this, our government made a clear decision: to restore the authority of the state without any ambiguity. Through the declaration of an internal armed conflict, our armed forces and national police, acting in a coordinated matter, are decisively confronting these groups, reaffirming the state's control over its territory and restoring the principle of authority. As the Ecuadorian government, we propose to strengthen cooperation on three specific fronts: reinforcing intelligence and interoperability capacities, modernizing border and port control using advanced technology, as well as a sustained support scheme that consolidates our institutional capacities in the long term. We need to keep in mind that it's not just about combating illicit shipments. It's about protecting our societies, our economies, and our future. Ecuador is willing to do its part. We hope to consolidate together with you, with all of you sitting at this table, a coordinated effort that ensures organized crime has no place in the Western Hemisphere. LIVE: Trump fires Kristi Noem as DHS secretary l ABC News Special Report

ABC News(19.4M) ·119:26 ·123.0K views · 2026-03-05
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Kristi Noem (Former DHS Secretary) Markwayne Mullin (U.S. Senator)
President Trump has fired Homeland Security Secretary Kristi Noem following a series of public controversies and "bruising" Congressional testimony, naming Oklahoma Senator Markwayne Mullin as her successor.
TL;DR President Trump has fired Homeland Security Secretary Kristi Noem following a series of public controversies and "bruising" Congressional testimony, naming Oklahoma Senator Markwayne Mullin as her successor. This marks the first major cabinet firing of Trump’s second term...

TL;DR

President Trump has fired Homeland Security Secretary Kristi Noem following a series of public controversies and "bruising" Congressional testimony, naming Oklahoma Senator Markwayne Mullin as her successor. This marks the first major cabinet firing of Trump’s second term and comes as the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) faces a partial funding shutdown and elevated threats from the conflict with Iran.

Key Factors Leading to Noem’s Removal

  • Disastrous Hill Testimony: The "last straw" for the President was Noem’s recent testimony before Senator John Kennedy (R-LA). Trump was reportedly "mad as a murder hornet" over her performance and her handling of questions regarding agency spending.
  • $220 Million Ad Campaign: Noem drew bipartisan ire for a taxpayer-funded ad campaign starring herself.
      • False Authorization: Noem suggested the President had authorized the expenditure; however, the White House later clarified that the President did not approve the use of funds for the campaign.
  • Minneapolis Shooting Response: Noem faced severe backlash for her response to the killing of two American citizens, Renee Good and Alex Pretti, by federal agents.
      • Misleading Claims: She labeled the victims "domestic terrorists" and falsely claimed Pretti was brandishing a gun, a claim later debunked by video evidence.
  • Operational Missteps: White House officials recently had to intervene to reverse Noem’s decision to pause TSA PreCheck during the current government shutdown.

The Appointment of Senator Markwayne Mullin

  • Background and Profile: Senator Mullin (R-OK) is a member of the Cherokee Nation, a former MMA fighter, and served 10 years in the House before joining the Senate in 2022.
  • Political Standing: Mullin is described as a "firebrand" and a fierce defender of the President. Unlike Noem, he is highly respected by Republican allies on the Hill, which may ease his upcoming confirmation battle.
  • Mullin’s Reaction: Mullin stated he was surprised by the call, which came through the White House switchboard, but is "super excited" to take the role and enforce Congressional laws.
  • Noem’s New Role: Kristi Noem will remain at DHS through the end of March before transitioning to a new position as Special Envoy for the Shield of the Americas, a security initiative focused on the Western Hemisphere.

DHS Operational Crisis and National Security

  • Partial Funding Shutdown: DHS has been partially shut down for three weeks as Democrats withhold funding to force reforms in ICE tactics. This has resulted in some DHS employees—including those protecting former presidents—working without pay.
  • The "Rot" in DHS: Senate Majority Leader Chuck Schumer (D-NY) reacted to the news by stating there is a "rot" throughout the agency that a change in leadership alone may not fix.
  • Iran Conflict Vulnerability: Security analysts warned that the leadership transition occurs at the "absolute worst time" given the ongoing "Operation Epic Fury" against Iran.
      • Elevated Threats: Concerns are rising regarding Iranian sleeper cells or proxies within the U.S. while the primary agency responsible for domestic security is underfunded and in transition.

Broader Geopolitical Context: Iran and Regime Change

  • Military Escalation: The Pentagon released video of a U.S. submarine sinking an Iranian warship, the first such attack since WWII.
  • Leadership Intervention: President Trump has stated he must be involved in the appointment of Iran’s next leader, dismissing the Ayatollah's son as a "lightweight."
  • Regime Change Confusion: Military analysts expressed concern over a "fuzzy definition" of U.S. objectives, questioning whether the mission is narrow military strikes or full regime change.

Notable Quotes

"The President, when he called me Tuesday night... was mad as a murder hornet. He asked me what I thought about Markwayne [Mullin] and I said: A, I like him; B, he's very smart; C, he's a very good businessman." — Senator John Kennedy (R-LA)
"There is a rot inside the Department of Homeland Security and just changing the leader at the top doesn't necessarily change the kind of tactics... that we've seen this DHS carry out." — Senator Chuck Schumer (D-NY)
"I wasn't expecting the call today but [I'm] super excited and I'm more excited about just ready to get started. There's a lot of work we can do to get Department of Homeland Security working for the American people." — Senator Markwayne Mullin (R-OK)
"It is important to remember that this is now the first major firing that we have seen in the president's second term... she is now the first cabinet secretary to be let go by President Trump." — Mary Bruce, ABC News Chief White House Correspondent

Pierre, as we know, this was created not long after 9/11. Osama bin Laden was able to work with his fellow terrorists onto our homeland and create a devastation that changed our lives forever. We had to do something different, which is why this department was created. Now, fast forward all these years later, it has been remarkable through the decades what the department has been able to do and implement to help keep our homeland safe. The Department of Homeland Security and the Homeland Security Secretary is one of the most important jobs in all of the federal government. They oversee how the airports are secured every time you get on a plane. They oversee the protection of the president of the United States. They oversee the Secret Service. They are responsible for developing the strategies to deal with the border and to deal with immigration. Fed's Kashkari Says War Creates Uncertainty for Rate Path (Full Interview from Invest)

Bloomberg Television(3.0M) ·23:37 ·1.4K views · 2026-03-05
Neel Kashkari (President, Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis)
Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis President Neel Kashkari indicates that while inflation is generally trending toward the 2% target, new geopolitical uncertainties and tariff-related price pressures have complicated the path for interest rate cuts,...
TL;DR Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis President Neel Kashkari indicates that while inflation is generally trending toward the 2% target, new geopolitical uncertainties and tariff-related price pressures have complicated the path for interest rate cuts, leaving him with a...

TL;DR

Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis President Neel Kashkari indicates that while inflation is generally trending toward the 2% target, new geopolitical uncertainties and tariff-related price pressures have complicated the path for interest rate cuts, leaving him with a projection of only one rate reduction for the remainder of the year.

Inflation Outlook and Geopolitical Shocks

  • Geopolitical Uncertainty: Kashkari is monitoring whether recent oil price spikes will mirror the massive commodity shock of the Russia-Ukraine invasion or the more contained impact of the Hamas-Israel conflict.
      • Implication: A prolonged spike in headline inflation could eventually bleed into core inflation as energy costs filter into manufacturing and services.
  • Core Inflation Components:
      • Housing: High confidence that this is trending down as new lease data eventually reflects in official statistics.
      • Core Goods: Currently trending up, largely attributed to the impact of tariffs and supply chain adjustments.
      • Services: Slowly trending down, closely tied to a cooling labor market.
  • Tariff Impact: The Supreme Court ruling on tariffs has introduced uncertainty. While Kashkari hasn't seen evidence of a "new round" of elevated inflation, he notes that core goods inflation has climbed linearly rather than remaining in its historical disinflationary trend.

Monetary Policy and the "Neutral Rate"

  • Rate Cut Projections: Kashkari’s "dot" in the Summary of Economic Projections (SEP) currently accounts for only one interest rate cut in 2024.
  • The Neutral Rate: Kashkari suggests the neutral rate (the rate at which policy is neither stimulative nor restrictive) is likely higher than previously thought.
      • Evidence: The U.S. economy has "powered through" aggressive rate hikes without the expected slowdown, suggesting that current policy is not as restrictive as modeled.
  • The 1990s Comparison: He references the Greenspan era, noting that even during a productivity boom, the Fed held rates at roughly 5%. He cautioned against the idea that productivity gains automatically necessitate immediate rate cuts.

Labor Market Dynamics and AI

  • Signs of Softening: For the first time in 10 years, businesses in North Dakota (traditionally worker-short) report being fully staffed, suggesting a "low hire, low fire" environment and cooling organic demand for labor.
  • AI and Capital Reallocation: Kashkari views AI as a "massively capital-intensive" technology.
      • Macro Impact: In the near term, AI may push the neutral rate higher as capital is reallocated from sectors like housing into data centers and compute infrastructure.
      • Business Adoption: CEOs report using AI for specific efficiency pockets, which is beginning to influence their long-term hiring decisions.

Fed Leadership and Institutional Change

  • Kevin Warsh Nomination: Kashkari expressed openness to Warsh’s potential leadership, noting his deep knowledge of the institution.
  • Balance Sheet Debate: Kashkari is willing to revisit the debate between an "ample reserves" regime and a "scarce reserves" regime, noting that the current system is not as simple as originally intended.
  • Communication Policy: Regarding Warsh’s criticism of frequent Fed official speeches, Kashkari defended the practice as essential for transparency and democratic accountability to regional constituents.
  • Jay Powell’s Status: If a successor is not confirmed by the end of Powell’s term, the Board of Governors would likely vote to have Powell serve as "Chair Pro Tem" to ensure continuity.
"The fact that the US economy has been this resilient given the stance of our monetary policy tells me the neutral rate must be higher, at least in the near term." — Neel Kashkari
"For [low-income] members, inflation is worse than a recession... in a recession, you rely on a brother or sister who still has a job. But inflation affects everybody, so there’s nobody in your network you can lean on for help." — Neel Kashkari (quoting a labor union leader)
"I thought when we raised rates as aggressively as we did that we’d be slamming the brakes on the economy. We weren’t." — Neel Kashkari
The president's war has obviously disrupted oil prices and they're way up over the last couple of days. That means gasoline prices go up, so that means inflation goes up. But the Fed has some experience dealing with this, right? So I think the question is, obviously when Russia invaded Ukraine, it sent a commodity shockwave all around the world. And I was on—go back a few years—I was on team transitory which said, "Hey, I think this inflation that's coming out of the pandemic won't be that bad and will be short-lived." Obviously, we adjusted that view. That was not the right call and we adjusted. We raised rates aggressively, in part because of that commodity shockwave. That was one of the contributing factors for why inflation got so high. But now, if you fast-forward a few years to when Hamas attacked Israel, I was ready for a commodity shockwave that didn't come. And so now the question I think that we are wrestling with, and markets are wrestling with, is how long is this going to last, how bad is it going to get, and is it going to look more like Russia-Ukraine or is it going to look more like Hamas attacking Israel? That's going to have effects on monetary policy. I don't think anybody thinks when Russia invaded Ukraine and the inflation that resulted, that the Fed should have just ignored that. I mean, that would have been the true transitory call. It's a one-time effect; just look through it. I think right now it's just too soon to know what imprint this has on inflation and for how long. Well, there's a follow-on question to that in that we've seen a sort of hockey stick action in gasoline prices since gasoline futures went up and oil prices went up over the last 24 hours. It's also used oil in many other manufactured products, in pharmaceuticals, in plastics, etc. How long do we need to see elevated prices before this becomes not just a headline spike, but it gets into core inflation? Yeah, I mean, that's something we think about all the time. We obviously, you know Mike, we focus on core. Our target is headline inflation, but we focus on core because core is a better indicator of where headline is going.
