But even when you strip out food and energy, those do eventually affect core because just as you said, energy goes into producing core goods and services as well. So I think that process takes a while. For me though, the bigger issue is just if headline inflation is going to be elevated for an extended period of time, coming off of five years of elevated inflation. Boy, that's a scenario that we need to pay close attention to, and we have to pay attention to what happens to long-run inflation expectations as well. And so there's a lot to pay attention to right now. Coming into this moment, I was feeling pretty good. Inflation is still too high, but I feel like it's been slowly heading in the right direction. The labor market is pretty good, a little bit getting softer, but generally speaking, we're around the bands of our dual mandate. And so I felt like policy was in a pretty good place and we have the luxury of just letting it gradually glide back to neutral. Now we need to see, with this new shock—potentially a new shock hitting the global economy—how long is the effect and how big is the effect. Well, as Rosanne Rosannadanna used to say, it's always something. And years ago before we started the war, we were worried about tariffs. Remember tariffs? The consensus of the Open Market Committee seems to be, listening to people's speeches, that we're kind of in peak tariffs now and you've basically said the same thing. Inflation should begin to come down in the second half of the year. But since the Supreme Court ruling, has that changed in your view in terms of we're not going to know what tariffs are for a while now and we're not going to know when they're going to put them on or how they're going to affect the economy, so you can't really have confidence that inflation is going to come down? Well, I think there's no question that the Supreme Court ruling and now the uncertainty about what is the new tariff regime, which authorities are they going to use, and how close can they get back to replicating what they had originally imposed—that’s introduced uncertainty. And uncertainty is a drag on the economy broadly. I haven't seen any evidence that the new tariff regime, whatever it is, is going to lead to a new round of elevated inflation. It seems like, based on the commentary from the administration, they mostly want to try to recreate what they had before and the question is how close can they come to that. So I would expect that inflation, just on that one factor, is likely to be on the same trajectory or maybe a somewhat lower trajectory if they're not able to fully replicate the tariffs that they had before. I haven't seen a lot of evidence that tariffs are going to go much higher from here. But you know, we need to see. Well, does this push out the timeframe for you to be able to have confidence that we're not seeing a tariff impact? I don't, just on the tariff effect, setting aside geopolitics and Iran, just on the tariff effects, it isn't obvious to me that it's going to delay the expected deceleration of inflation because I don't see a lot of scenarios where they're going to substantially increase tariffs from here. So where do you put yourself now? Obviously, you have to put a new dot plot together for the next meeting on March 18th. What do you think? Do you think, first of all, there's going to be any move in March? I would assume you would say no. But after that, when do you think it's more likely that you might consider it as a live issue? Well, in December, if you look at my December SEP, I had preferred us not cutting in December but the committee did. If you just do the math, I effectively had one dot now for 2026. I basically had two and two, and because they did three, then I brought one dot forward. So that leaves me with one dot coming into recent events, so one expected cut later on this year. Now with the geopolitical events that we talked about, I just need to see. We need to get a lot more data in. The inflation data before Iran, I think, has suggested that inflation is running somewhere between two and a half and three percent. I think housing inflation is trending down, I've got a lot of confidence in that. Core goods inflation has been trending up because of tariffs. Services inflation, I think, is slowly trending down tied to the labor market. And the labor market is in a decent place but showing some signs of softness. And so that's what gave me confidence that, hey, one cut later this year might make sense. Now the neutral rate—I don't know, well tell us where you think we are compared to neutral because the question I have is every SEP says that the Fed will hit its target two years from now. And the next SEP says two years from now. Are you ever going to really hit it? Well, we have to hit it. And I think my colleagues and I are committed, for the credibility of the institution and for anchored inflation expectations, that we absolutely have to hit it. But where the neutral rate is has been a big question mark. Over the last several years, I kept forecasting that the US economy is going to slow down and then it just powered through. I thought when we raised rates as aggressively as we did that we'd be slamming the brakes on the economy. We weren't. The economy proved to be much more resilient in the face of tariffs. It created a lot of uncertainty, created a lot of noise, raised goods inflation went up, but the US economy proved more resilient. And so the fact that the US economy has been this resilient given the stance of our monetary policy tells me the neutral rate must be higher, at least in the near term. And the thing about the dot plot, which is a very imperfect communication device, is that the long-run dot is meant to represent where's the long-run neutral after all of the shocks in the economy have passed and everything has settled back down to normal. That doesn't necessarily tell us that much about where is neutral today. We don't know when the shocks will ever stop under this administration. Correct. At the last meeting, and you touched on this just a second ago, the focus of the committee's concern seemed to shift away from the labor market back to inflation. Are the risks now balanced? They feel more balanced. Again, before setting aside the recent geopolitical events which we need to understand, the inflation and labor market do feel more balanced. I'll give you a quick anecdote. I go out and I travel around my district which is Minnesota, North and South Dakota, Montana, part of Michigan and northwestern Wisconsin. North Dakota is always structurally short of workers. So for 10 years I've been going to North Dakota and the number one issue I hear from businesses large and small is we can't find enough workers. I was in North Dakota a couple weeks ago, a roundtable of small businesses, and roughly half the businesses said we're fully staffed. That is the first time in 10 years I've heard that from businesses in North Dakota. Part of the story of why there's been no job creation is because there's been very little immigration into this country. But anecdotes like the North Dakota anecdote suggest to me there's also not a lot of underlying organic demand for labor right now. So I do see signs. Yes, 4.3 percent unemployment rate is still a good unemployment rate, but I do see evidence that the labor market is not demanding a lot more workers right now. So I take that and I try to balance that against the fact that inflation is still too high, but I do believe it's gently trending down. Well, you said you travel around talking to businesses around the district of course, and I'm wondering what they're telling you about this "low hire, low fire" environment. Is it because they can't find workers that they're not hiring, or is it because of all the uncertainty that they're not hiring? Are they finding that they can get the job done with the staff that they have and therefore we have a new dynamic? I think it's the latter two. I think there remains a lot of domestic uncertainty and global uncertainty, the topics you all are well aware of, and they are finding they can get by with what they have. And every large business that I talk to, I ask them specifically, "Are you using AI for real?" Not just some little pilot, are you actually using it? And virtually every business says yes. Even businesses that two years ago said that they were skeptical, that consultants were pitching them AI and they aren't even sure how they could use it, they give me real specific examples. Now, it's not transforming their whole business, but there are pockets of their business where they've been able to put it to work and do things that they couldn't do before or do things much more efficiently. And I do think that that is affecting their decisions on how many people they want to hire given we are early in this adoption phase. Staying with AI, because you brought it up, there is an argument that's being made by some people that you would be able to have lower interest rates because AI will increase productivity. There's also an argument that AI will lead to a higher neutral rate because of investment and/or demand rising. Where do you come down? I mean, I think if you have a massive new technology, AI is not a low-capital technology. This is massively capital-intensive with hundreds of billions of dollars or maybe trillions of dollars going to build data centers and compute things you all understand. How does that happen? All right, the economy reallocates capital to its most productive use through higher rates. And that means apartment buildings that would have been built absent AI, that capital is now going to shift over and build data centers because mortgage rates are going to be higher than they otherwise would have been. That's how capital gets reallocated in the market economy. And so, could it have a long-term effect on the neutral rate and bring things down? It's possible. In the near term, do I think it's probably pushing up the neutral rate? Yes, I think so likely. And you know, people make comparisons to the 90s and Greenspan famously saying, "Hey, there's a productivity boom." It wasn't a debate about cutting rates; it was a debate about whether they should be raising rates, and Greenspan used the productivity insight to say let's just hold them where they are. By the way, that was about a 5 percent federal funds rate. And so when people look to the 90s, I think that there are lessons to be learned from the 90s, but I don't look at that and say, "Oh, that means we should be cutting rates right now" when the policy rate is well below where it was in the 90s. What are the CEOs telling you about pricing? Are they sitting on the need to raise prices? Are they going to be raising prices because of tariffs? Where are they? The most common comment that I've heard in recent months is you would expect to see some repricing at the start of the year. They were reluctant to pass on costs to their customers last year, they wanted to try to adjust their supply chains or adjust their product mix to avoid having to pass that pain on, but the longer it goes eventually they will have to pass it on. The start of the year would be a natural inflection point. And so I think we are starting to see some evidence of continued pass-through in this early inflation data. But I'm not hearing a lot of anecdotes that they see major inflation waves yet to come. It's more that things are slowly adjusting to the effect of tariffs from last year. So this is still a "look-through-it" situation. Well, it's a "pay-attention-to-it" situation. I mean yes, we're trying to get a sense of where we think inflation is headed, but we can't be completely dismissive of the fact that consumers are feeling the prices. If you look at the goods inflation, core goods inflation is real. It should be negative in a normal American economy in recent years. It was negative briefly and then it has climbed linearly since then, and so that's real and that's having an effect on the economy. What's your forecast for inflation this year then? The progression, I assume you end the year lower than we are now. Yeah, somewhat lower than we are now, and I'm more focused on the trajectory than I am on whether we're going to print 2.5 or 2.2, but generally lowering. Again, I go through the fundamental components. Housing inflation I have a lot of confidence is trending down because we understand the mechanism from new rents and new leases. After a year or two, it translates into the official housing statistics. New rents and new leases have come way down. So I've got a lot of confidence that housing inflation is coming down. That's a big piece of the basket. Then you've got core goods, which I talked about. They had already returned to slight disinflation, which is normal in the US economy, but climbed because of tariffs. That ought to be a short-lived event unless there's new tariff effects brewing. And then you have core services outside of housing, and that ought to be tied to wages. If the labor market is slowing—and I think it is—that ought to be coming down. So that's the reason I've got some confidence that inflation is headed in the right direction but for recent events, which have now introduced a new shock that we need to pay attention to. And yet here we are. So how much confidence do you have in the forecasts that you're going to be putting out in March? I mean, I had a lot of confidence up until a couple days ago that just like the dynamic that I described of where inflation is headed, I had more confidence. And so now back to where we started: does this look more like Russia invading Ukraine or does this end up looking more like Hamas attacking Israel? I don't know yet, and so we need to wait and see. So let's shift gears a little bit here and talk about the new boss. For you Who fans out there, not the same as the old boss. It might surprise the President, but the Fed chair just can't walk in and lower interest rates on their own. The main power the chair has is persuasion. So how do you think Kevin Warsh will do with that? I think he'll do well. I mean, he's obviously spent five or six years as a governor at the Federal Reserve Board so he knows the institution well. I think he's been focused on and studying these issues for a long time. And I think he will come forward with—you know, he's talked about a wide range of things in between when he was at the board and now, everything from monetary policy to how we implement monetary policy to how we're organized. I think those are all great topics and I look forward to hearing what his priorities are and then engaging in the discussion. He hasn't been exactly complimentary about the Fed in the last couple of years. How's that going to go over? How does that go through the system? I think it'll be fine. I mean, I think look, we've made mistakes. We're not perfect. I think we are all doing our very best in a highly uncertain environment. I've said I was on team transitory. I thought that inflation coming out of COVID would be short-lived and not that bad. I was wrong about that. And so I think we should be forced to step back and look at what have we done well, what did we miss, why did we miss it, and how do we do better going forward. Now, assuming he is confirmed and in place on time, how long will it be before people like me are referring to it as the Warsh Fed in the same way that people ask when did it become the Trump economy? When do you think people will look at it as him as the centerpiece driving the Fed? Boy, I hadn't thought about that. I mean, that'll be for—that'll be your call because you're going to be writing the headlines or your editors will be writing the headlines on top of your stories. You know, I don't know. But people are going to take his views seriously the moment he shows up, the moment he engages in debate and makes—I mean, the chair has a lot of influence. The chair can say, "Hey, here are the range of options I want the committee to consider," and then we have deliberations around those options. And so I think the day he shows up and starts setting the agenda for the FOMC, in a way it becomes his Fed. Well, one of the things he wants to do is have a smaller balance sheet. Now whether that means you go back to scarce reserves or just a smaller balance sheet, where would you come down on that idea? I think it's timely to have that debate. You know, when I first got to the Fed 10 years ago, I asked the question, "Why did the Fed switch from a scarce reserves regime to an ample regime?" And one of the explanations I was given is that, well, an ample regime is much simpler. Instead of the New York desk having to trade in and out of markets to keep the federal funds rate in the range, you just set a rate, you dial it in, and you forget it. Sounds great in theory. In reality, we do that. We also have a ceiling facility called the discount window, we have a separate ceiling facility called the supplemental facility, and we also have a floor facility called the ONRRP. Once in a while we have to make technical adjustments on the rates to keep things in the range, and then once in a while the New York desk still has to intervene and inject reserves into the market. So this very simple regime that we've created is not nearly as simple as it was originally explained. And so for us to step back and look at that and say, "Is this optimal for the US economy?" I think that that would be a debate that would be well worth having. He also thinks that y'all talk too much and he has suggested he'd like to see fewer and more coordinated speeches and essays. You've been at the forefront of communicating through all kinds of means. So should he clip your wings? Well, I don't know what you're going to do all day if he does that. Here's the challenge. So, just—this is my approach to communicating. When I go out across my district, we get lots of invitations from chambers of commerce and universities and rotary clubs. I turn down the vast majority of those, but I do want to visit every state in my district at least once a year and just communicate what it is that we're looking at at the Fed. Most of the time I'm not seeking to make news, I'm just explaining, "Hey, this is what's going on in the economy, this is what we looked at, this is why the committee made the decision that they made at the last meeting." Now, I also live-stream these events in the spirit of transparency so I'm not shedding secrets. And yet me doing that can lead to some headlines. And now when you have 12 presidents doing that across their districts, it's good for democracy because we're communicating to our constituents. But I can also see how people say, "Well, it generates a lot of headlines on certain news outlets," and so I don't know how to change that if you want to be transparent to the American public and transparent to the communities that we are charged to represent. That's the part that makes it hard. As far as we know, Kevin Warsh hasn't been formally nominated yet and then he has to go through the nomination hearings. We have one senator at least who says he's going to block everything until the investigation into Jay Powell is finished. In your understanding, what happens if Warsh is not confirmed by the time the chair loses his chairmanship, not his seat on the board? Well, this happened between Jay Powell's first and second terms and it happened sometime in the Greenspan era too, where there were delays—not through any big problem, but there was just a delay in the White House acting and the Senate acting. And my understanding is the board of governors votes at that moment to then re-extend the current chair to serve as chair pro tem. So it's just an extension, so to speak, of the role at the board of governors and that'll be up for the governors to sort out, but that's the precedent. Well, the interesting thing is you vote every year who's going to be the chair of the Open Market Committee and this year you voted Jay Powell to be the chair until next year. But in that past episode they just voted to have him be the chair until a successor is in place. Was the board trying to send a message? I'm unaware. I mean, every year I'm used to—every year we have a vote on who the chair of the FOMC is going to be for that year, and this just seemed like the routine process that we followed every year. So I'm unaware if there was some implied message attempting to be sent. Would you like to see Jay Powell stay on as a governor? I think Jay Powell's done a terrific job as a governor and as chair and I'd love it if he chose to continue because I think he's a great colleague. We'll finish up with an audience question here. If employment continues to soften and inflation doesn't come down, which is worse for America? It's hard. You know, this is a pickle. I'll give you a quick example. Conventional economic analysis says that recessions—so high unemployment—are devastating and worse than inflation. But when I actually talk to workers—like I talked to a head of—I've talked about this publicly before—I've talked to a head of a local labor union in Minnesota, one who represents low-income service workers. And she made the opposite comment. She said for her members, inflation is worse than a recession. And I said, "I don't understand that. In a recession you lose your job; in inflation you just pay more." She said, "Because my members are used to recessions. They rely on a brother, a sister, a mom or dad, a friend who still has a job to help them get through it. But inflation affects everybody, so there's nobody in your network you can lean on for help because they're all suffering like you are." That was a profound insight that I was not ready for and it made me step back and reassess my judgments about these things. So I'm going to give you an "I don't know" answer. We got to get inflation back down to 2 percent and we need to keep the labor market strong. So all these people can rush out at lunch and trade, no move in March? I'll let you speculate about that.

LIVE: Pete Hegseth and Stephen Miller speak at Americas Counter Cartel Conference

Associated Press(4.3M) ·32:24 ·10.9K views · 2026-03-05
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of Defense) Stephen Miller (White House Advisor)
The Trump administration is shifting to a "War Department" footing in the Western Hemisphere, utilizing lethal military force to eradicate cartels—classified as the "ISIS of the West"—and re-establishing the Monroe Doctrine to secure "Greater North America.
TL;DR The Trump administration is shifting to a "War Department" footing in the Western Hemisphere, utilizing lethal military force to eradicate cartels—classified as the "ISIS of the West"—and re-establishing the Monroe Doctrine to secure "Greater North America." From Law...

TL;DR

The Trump administration is shifting to a "War Department" footing in the Western Hemisphere, utilizing lethal military force to eradicate cartels—classified as the "ISIS of the West"—and re-establishing the Monroe Doctrine to secure "Greater North America."

Military Paradigm Shift: Cartels as Terrorist Entities

  • From Law Enforcement to Lethal Force: Stephen Miller and Pete Hegseth declared that the "criminal justice" approach to cartels has failed. The administration is now utilizing "hard power" and military assets to dismantle organizations.
      • Terrorist Classification: Cartels are explicitly likened to ISIS and Al-Qaeda; Miller argued they must be treated with the same "brutality" and "ruthlessness" as those groups.
      • The "War Department": Hegseth highlighted the restoration of the "Department of War" title (formerly Defense), signaling a return to a "warrior ethos" and the primary mission of finding, fixing, and finishing enemies.
  • Critique of Soft Power: Miller dismissed the advice of NGOs and "lawyers," claiming that Western civilization was built by first "eradicating criminal elements" through "ruthless means" to establish order.

The "Donroe Doctrine" and Hemispheric Defense

  • Monroe Doctrine Revival: The administration is re-asserting the 200-year-old Monroe Doctrine, colloquially referred to by Hegseth as the "Donroe Doctrine," to prevent external adversaries (like China or Russia) from interfering in the hemisphere.
  • Greater North America Strategic Map: Hegseth defined a new security perimeter called "Greater North America," encompassing every sovereign territory north of the equator (from Greenland to Ecuador).
      • Quarter Sphere Defense: This strategy prioritizes U.S. posture in the North while requiring "burden-sharing" from partners south of the equator to secure the South Atlantic and Pacific.
  • Rejection of Globalism: The speakers rejected "globalism" and "narco-communism," arguing that national identity and strong borders are the only path to regional prosperity.

Operational Results and Border Militarization

  • Active Border Control: Hegseth confirmed that the 101st Airborne Division currently has "operational control" of the U.S. southern border, treating migration and smuggling as a core military function rather than just law enforcement.
  • Offensive Operations:
      • Operation Absolute Resolve: Hegseth cited a January raid in Caracas that led to the arrest of Nicolás Maduro and his wife, identifying them as fugitives of U.S. law.
      • Operation Southern Sphere: A maritime offensive targeting narco-terrorist logistics; Hegseth noted a 56% drop in fentanyl flows and a significant reduction in cartel boat activity due to established military deterrence.
  • Human Smuggling Statistics: Hegseth noted that under the previous administration, the human smuggling industry grew by 2,000%, from a $500 million industry in 2018 to $13 billion by 2022.

Restoring Military Unity and Sovereignty

  • Unity Over Diversity: Hegseth explicitly rejected the phrase "diversity is our strength," calling it "the single dumbest phrase in military history." He argued that military effectiveness relies on unity, shared purpose, and a singular "warrior ethos."
  • Economic Security Cycle: Miller argued that lack of security leads to "human capital" flight; illegal immigration facilitates a cycle where cartels grow rich on smuggling fees while neighboring nations are drained of their citizens and wealth.
  • Call for Regional Action: The conference served as a directive for Latin American partners to militarize their own counter-cartel efforts, with Hegseth stating the U.S. is prepared to act alone but prefers "aggressive" regional collaboration.

***

Notable Quotes

"The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations." — Stephen Miller
"The reason why this is a conference with military leadership and not a conference of lawyers is because these organizations can only be defeated with military power... You have my permission not to listen to [the lawyers]." — Stephen Miller
"Under previous leaders we grew obsessed with every other theater and every other border in the world except our own. These elites reduced our power and presence in this hemisphere, opting for a benign neglect that was anything but benign." — Pete Hegseth
"One of the dumbest phrases in military history... was 'our diversity is our strength.' It’s the single dumbest phrase in military history. Because... our strength is our unity." — Pete Hegseth
Thank you. I would now like to invite Deputy Chief of Staff for Policy and Assistant to the President for Homeland Security, Stephen Miller, to the podium. Sir, the floor is yours. Thank you distinguished guests. Thank you General Donovan. Thank you General Northouse. And thank you to my good friend Secretary Hegseth, whom I’ve long admired, but I’ve gotten to know personally in the last number of months. He is as fine a gentleman and as fierce a warrior as you will ever meet. We’ve all learned to put our faith and confidence in his leadership of the War Department, so thank you, Pete. There’s been a paradigm shift under President Trump in the Western Hemisphere in the last 14 months. Some of that was touched on by the earlier speaker, but just to restate it: we are not going to cede an inch of territory in this hemisphere to our enemies or our adversaries. Our national security, our homeland security, the safety and well-being of our people begins at home, begins in our neighborhood, and begins in our home region. For too long, we have allowed foreign enemies, foreign adversaries, and enemy terrorist organizations to control territory and spaces in this hemisphere where they can project power, project threats, and directly threaten the lives of your citizens and our citizens. Under the leadership of President Trump, we are using hard power, military power, and lethal force to protect and defend the American homeland. Not a single one of your nations should tolerate the existence of a single square mile of territory that is under the control of any entity other than the sovereign governments of your country. The idea that we would have, in this hemisphere, areas that are under the physical control of foreign terrorist organizations or paramilitary organizations is completely unacceptable. The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations. What we have learned after decades of effort is that there is not a criminal justice solution to the cartel problem. There are elements of the problem that require a criminal justice solution, to be sure, but just as we fought Al-Qaeda and fought ISIS with the tip of a very lethal spear, these organizations can only be defeated with military power. The reason why this is a conference with military leadership and not a conference of lawyers is because these organizations can only be defeated with military power. I see some heads nodding up front because they understand. You're dealing with a lot of lawyers in your own country, I'm sure. You have my permission not to listen to them. The key condition of human flourishing and of prosperity is order and security. A country cannot pursue economic growth, cultural enrichment, art, architecture, education, or science if it doesn’t have fundamental security first. For too long, we’ve been caught in a vicious cycle in this hemisphere where countries from Mexico down to the tip of South America do not provide their citizens with basic physical security. So those citizens, in search of economic opportunity, illegally immigrate to the United States. They pay the smugglers and pay the cartels, who then grow richer and more powerful. Your countries, in turn, become poor because you’ve lost your citizens, you’ve lost your human capital, and you’ve lost your opportunities to develop and to grow. So you get poor, the cartels get more powerful, and nothing changes, nothing improves. You have to break that cycle. You have to completely separate, and that begins by dismantling these organizations that deal drugs, deal violence, assassinate, extort, blackmail, and also traffic illegally in human beings. That is a form of terrorism too. Illegal immigration facilitated by criminal networks is a form of terrorism. Those who are stealing your children and stealing your families and stealing your human capital are part of that terrorist continuum and should be thought of as such. It is not a victimless crime when people are paying human smuggling organizations, because that money then fuels assassinations, it fuels violence, it fuels instability, and it continues this cycle. For decades, a lot of organizations were sent to your countries. These are known as NGOs, I think you know the term non-governmental organizations. They gave you very, very bad advice for a very, very long time. Basically they told you, in short, that you needed to be softer and gentler and nicer to the criminals, and that you had to demilitarize your approach and extend more rights and more opportunities to the people who were sabotaging and destroying your society. I would make the point to you, as others have pointed out before, including the President of El Salvador, that the West only achieved the place that it did in human history because it spent previous centuries eradicating the criminal elements within its territories. The West that we came to know—that had the great music and architecture and science and the most powerful economy—it had spent centuries previous to that establishing order first. So that by the time you get, you know, to 18th-century England or 19th-century England, there had been centuries of previous history in which ruthless means were used to get rid of the people who were raping and murdering and defying established systems of order and justice. Then when people could live freely and peaceably and children and families and women could be safe, then it became possible to pursue all of these higher elements of life. So when people are coming to countries like yours that have had struggles and difficulties and pains and challenges and they’re telling you anything other than first and foremost secure your countries and your territory, they are giving you not only terrible advice but advice that if someone had come to Europe centuries ago and they had followed that advice, Europe would have never became the Europe that it eventually became. The reason why many Western countries are struggling today is they’ve forgotten the eternal truths and wisdoms they once followed. And that’s why here under President Trump in America, we are returning to those fundamental truths. We’re returning to those fundamental principles. I’ll just close by saying that you will have in President Trump in the White House, in the War Department, the Department of State, and the whole of our government, unwavering support, unwavering commitment, and fidelity in achieving this mission, which is, very simply, to eradicate the criminal cartels and terrorist organizations, to deprive them of control of weapons, spaces, institutions, and territory. The human rights that we are going to protect are not of the savages that torture and rape and murder. The human rights that we are going to protect are of the peaceable citizens who have an absolute human right to live in physical safety and security every day of their lives. And in that mission, I promise you, we will be your closest partner. Thank you. Ladies and gentlemen, it is now my distinct pleasure to introduce our Secretary of War, the Honorable Pete Hegseth. Well, good morning everybody. General Donovan and I are pleased to welcome all of you to Southern Command this morning. I’m honored to be the first Secretary of War to lead a hemispheric defense conference like this in more than 30 years. This conference is about you. This conference is about us. This conference is not called the America apostrophe S, America’s Counter-Cartel Conference. It’s the Americas Counter-Cartel Conference. This is about what we together can do. You know, it’s been more than 200 years since our nations struggled for independence from foreign rule. The War of 1812 was about the United States defending its independence and protecting its territory. A 47-year-old Carolinian who was a major general in the army, as was mentioned, Andrew Jackson, defeated a British army almost double the size. Addressing his troops after victory at the Battle of New Orleans, Jackson declared: "Natives of different states acting together for the first time have reaped the fruits of an honorable union." His success galvanized a young country. In 1816 our country elected a Revolutionary War hero as President. James Monroe fought in the Continental Army. Leading a charge against German mercenaries fighting for the British, the enemy fired a musket ball at him. It severed his artery and he almost died. But the young captain rallied and grew to be Governor of Virginia, Secretary of War, and one of our greatest presidents. In 1823, President Monroe said that the United States owed the success of that young country to the candor and amicable relations with other powers to affirm our national policy. President Monroe declared: "We should consider any attempt on malign countries to extend their system to any portion of this hemisphere as dangerous to our peace and safety." It was the Monroe Doctrine of 1823. And today, some 200 years later, we still marvel at the wisdom of President Monroe’s declaration. We, like you, want borders and sovereign territories that are secure. We want unfettered access to key terrain and trade so that our nations can industrialize. And we want to prevent external powers from threatening our peace and independence in our shared neighborhood. This is the essence of the Monroe Doctrine. No external power will interfere in this hemisphere. Ours should be a region of strong sovereign nations. It’s the same principle that animates President Trump’s defense approach today. President Trump and his Department of War are ushering in a new era of homeland and hemispheric defense, rooted in the unique heritage of our nation and your nations. Under President Trump, we restored the Department of War. You see, the Department of War was established by General George Washington and served our nation from its founding through our hard-fought victory in World War II. Many of your countries also once had ministries of war. Under President Trump, our nation has a newfound resolve that our founders would recognize, as Stephen alluded to and spoke to. They would recognize it and they would appreciate it. We are at long last putting America, Americans, and the Americas first, ensuring peace through strength and restoring common sense. At our department, we're doing it by restoring the warrior ethos, rebuilding our military to historic levels, and reestablishing deterrence in this hemisphere and around the world. For too long leaders in Washington and their foreign policy functionaries abandoned the simple wisdom of the Monroe Doctrine. They were consumed with the United States only going abroad. They ignored the warnings of President John Quincy Adams who said that if our nation went overseas only in search of monsters to destroy, that we would cease to be the ruler of our own spirit and our own backyard. Under previous leaders we grew obsessed with every other theater and every other border in the world except our own. These elites reduced our power and presence in this hemisphere, opting for a benign neglect that was anything but benign. In your countries, many leaders accepted the status quo to coexist with narco-terrorism or for a law enforcement, for a law enforcement alone approach that failed to deter and dismantle threats. Same happened in our country under the previous administration. The result of this collective neglect was fatal. More than 1 million Americans were killed by fentanyl, cocaine, and other drugs. These are numbers greater than any casualties we’ve sustained in war. Millions of illegal aliens invaded our borders and destabilized nations across the hemisphere. Under Joe Biden’s administration, the human smuggling industry exploded by more than 2,000 percent. It went from a 500-million-dollar so-called industry in 2018 to 13 billion by 2022. Cartels of terrorists across our hemisphere, enabled by adversaries, created and profited from chaos. What creates chaos? No leadership creates the chaos. In our nation, narco-terrorist groups like Tren de Aragua prospered. They raped, they poisoned, they murdered innocent Americans, and they terrorized our cities, our towns, and our neighborhood, like they do yours. Across this region these gangsters control and terrorize large swaths of your territories, kill thousands of your people, and promote chaos that makes normal life sometimes almost impossible. Crime spiked massively. This, our hemisphere, has one-eighth of the population yet one-third of the violent crime. There’s a reason for that. America’s retreat from protecting its citizens and the complacency of many neighbors in this hemisphere was a great betrayal. That’s what it was: a great betrayal against our people and our nations. Well, President Trump recognizes the wisdom of the Monroe Doctrine and the days of us betraying and endangering our own citizens are finished. President Trump has reestablished the Monroe Doctrine, the Trump Corollary of the Monroe Doctrine, or if you’d like for short, you can just call it the Donroe Doctrine. Under President Trump, securing America’s interest in the Western Hemisphere and keeping our homeland safe are our top national security priorities. The President’s historic national defense strategy ensures that the Department of War will prioritize resources around the threats and objectives that are core to homeland defense and the people and prosperity of the American people. To start, the Department of War is once again defending our borders as a priority of our national defense. With our law enforcement partners, we are defending our borders and making our streets safe again. Crossings at our southern border, at the U.S.-Mexico border, have dropped to the lowest number in history. Murders nationwide have plummeted by more than 20 percent. We now have operational control of our southern border. Zero people are crossing our southern border. We will not rest until we have complete operational control of every inch of all of our borders. So it starts with us. We're addressing it, but we're not stopping there. Under President Trump, for the first time in history, the Department of War is on the offense against narco-terrorists as well. I should back up a step, though. In order to get 100 percent operational control of our border, we didn’t just look at Border Patrol and say that’s your job, it’s a law enforcement function. We deployed the United States military. Right now, the division command of the 101st Airborne is in operational control of the southern border. We ensured that the most capable assets we had in our country were deployed to our own border to defend that border against the narco-terrorists and drug dealers seeking to enter our country. We are not treating it simply as a law enforcement function. We are working with our partners but recognizing it's a core military function. Going on offense with Operation Southern Sphere has restored deterrence against the narco-terrorist cartels that profited from poisoning Americans. Last month, we went a few weeks without targeting a single boat. Why? Well, because we couldn’t find a whole lot of boats to sink. And that’s the whole point: to establish deterrence from narco-terrorists who have been able to traffic almost unfettered. If the consequence was simply to be arrested and then released, well, that’s a consequence they’d already priced in a long time ago. A new dynamic had to be put in motion in order for their decision cycle to change. When adversaries are forced to move out of comfortable positions, as you know as military men and women, they’re more vulnerable when they’re moving in a different direction. Which is also the point. Drug overdoses that kill our citizens have dropped. Flows of fentanyl, a weapon of mass destruction, are down 56 percent. We will continue to destroy these threats. In January, U.S. forces raided and arrested the most dangerous house in the most dangerous compound inside the most fortified fort in the capital city of Caracas. They arrested two indicted individuals, Nicolás Maduro and his wife, fugitives of U.S. law. Operation Absolute Resolve showed the bravery of America’s best, our warfighters. Nobody can do what the United States of America can do. It’s not even close. President Trump has shown what is possible when you reject the status quo delusion that threats to our homeland and our hemisphere are somehow secondary. America is prepared to take on these threats and go on the offense alone if necessary. However, it is our preference and it is the goal of this conference that in the interest of this neighborhood, we all do it together with you, with our neighbors, and with our allies who are eager and willing and capable. To do this, to work together, we must first acknowledge what was lost and then understand what needs to be restored. All the nations represented in this room are offspring of Western civilization. Our nations are and always will be united by our heritage, our history, and geography in this new world. We share the same interests. Because of this, we face an essential test: whether our nations will be and remain Western nations, with distinct characteristics, as Christian nations under God. We must be proud of our shared heritage with strong borders and prosperous people ruled not by violence and chaos but by law, order, and common sense. Otherwise, we are permanently torn apart by something else, led astray by competing forces. Radical narco-communism and anarcho-tyranny threaten our people, borders, and sovereign lands in the name of a false sovereignty or a false peace. Uncontrolled mass migration overwhelms domestic resources intended for deserving citizens and drives unchecked crime and unchecked violence. Or there is the belief in so-called globalism that seeks to erase our distinct national identities in the name of tolerance. It seeks to erase our borders in the name of compassion and erase our warrior ethos and that which makes us strong in the name of so-called diversity and political correctness. One of the dumbest phrases in military history which used to be echoed from the former Defense Department in the previous administration—they would say it over and over and over again—was "our diversity is our strength." It’s the single dumbest phrase in military history. Because what is our strength? Our strength is our unity. Our strength is in our shared purpose, in the training we do together, and the capabilities we have that we can do together. In our context, it is the Constitution we swear an oath to defend together and the uniform we wear together. That is our strength. We reject other notions and offer a new choice. We must defend our national interest and restore our national identities. Otherwise the outcome is the same: our hemisphere will be poorer, more dangerous, and weaker. This matters not because we seek to preach to you about culture wars or ideologies or to tell you how to run your countries. We're not here to tell you how to run your countries. Instead, it matters how we assess the threats facing us and the choices that we make on how to overcome them. That is what will define our destiny. President Trump understands that today’s threats to border security and key terrain in our hemisphere are existential questions for our nation and for all of yours. When adversaries conduct incursions in this hemisphere off the coast of a U.S. state, Alaska, or off the coast of Greenland, or in the Gulf of America or the Caribbean, that is a direct threat to the United States homeland and to peace in this hemisphere. When adversaries control ports or infrastructure along strategic chokepoints for U.S. and hemispheric trade, such as the Panama Canal, or install military facilities just miles off our shore, that is a threat to the United States homeland and peace to this hemisphere. When terrorist killers and cartels capture strategic infrastructure, resources, and entire towns or cities close to U.S. borders and U.S. shores, or profit from mass illegal migration, that is a threat to the United States homeland and a threat to all of you as well, to the Americas. The same adversaries that threaten our shared heritage threaten our shared geography as well. They seek to displace the historic North-South relationship that we’ve always shared with some sort of a new global South that excludes the United States and other Western nations but includes non-Western powers and other adversaries. The answer to our challenge is not to ignore our geography in the name of global interests, but to embrace our shared geography in the name of national interests. That is why President Trump has drawn a new strategic map from Greenland to the Gulf of America to the Panama Canal and its surrounding countries. At the Department of War, we call this strategic map the Greater North America. Why? Because every sovereign nation and territory north of the equator from Greenland to Ecuador and from Alaska to Guyana is not part of the global South. It is our immediate security perimeter in this great neighborhood that we all live in. Each one of these countries borders either the North Atlantic or the North Pacific. Each one of these countries sits north of the two basic geographic barriers that exist in this region: the Amazon and the Andes mountains. This is basic geography that we don’t teach in schools as much as we should. It restores our North-South relations and we must get it right. In the north, the United States must enhance posture and presence in cooperation with you and our sovereign partners to defend our shared immediate security perimeter. In the south, meaning south of the equator, the other side of this great neighborhood, we will strengthen partnerships through increased burden-sharing. This will enable you to take your greater role to defend the South Atlantic and the South Pacific and to secure critical infrastructure and resources in partnership with us and other Western nations. This is what we did in World War II, just like we sunk ships with torpedoes in World War II. At the Department of War, we call it the Quarter Sphere Defense. And we will do this again. If we're serious about our national security and if we prioritize geography, business as usual will not stand. This means that for every country in this hemisphere, border security must be your top priority. Border security is national security. President Trump said it when he first started to run: you’re not a country if you don’t have borders. And he’s right. President Trump sealed our border by directing the military to mobilize resources and personnel to stop invasion by cartels and other criminal actors. You can do this as well, and many of you have. This also means that you too can and must go on the offense against narco-terrorists. We’ve had great collaboration with many countries in recent months and I want to thank every country in this room that has supported our access to find, fix, and finish cartel targets. We have only just begun to work with you. More must be done by you and by us to target narco-terrorist groups across all domains. We will dismantle narco-terrorist networks in this hemisphere and we will deny access to the state adversaries who support them. We will correct the mistakes of the past, the mistakes of this so-called global South. I want to thank General Donovan, who has been an excellent partner and a great new leader here at Southern Command. One of the great mistakes of the past was that our leaders didn’t provide the U.S. Southern Command with the support that it needed. It was a command chalk-full of lawyers and social workers and NGOs and law enforcement. That’s changing. General, I’m directing my team to correct these historic failures, to ensure that your command has the resources and staff that you need for this robust mission ahead. It’s the least we can do. I want to, absolutely, thank you. It’s about time. But it’s not just us at the Department of War and it’s not just Southern Command; this is across the Trump administration. Stephen Miller, thank you for your support, your leadership, and your clarity in understanding why this matters to each American citizen and why the War Department is central to ensuring that it happens. It doesn’t happen without President Trump and it doesn’t happen without your clarity and your leadership. I want to thank General Northouse as well for your support at every level. There’s so many to thank. I see Corey Lewandowski here from the Department of Homeland Security; it doesn’t happen without the Department of Homeland Security. Of course Joseph Humire, the other part of the new bromance between him and General Donovan. Eric Earcy, who from day one in my office—he was my first sergeant in Iraq and is one of my senior advisors—day one he worked at SOUTHCOM for a long time and said, "we need to be focused on the cartel threat." He built a team to help put us in that direction, a lot of which manifests to this day. Patrick Weaver, Phil Hegseth, whom I happen to know, and Ricky Barea, my chief, for doing a great job putting this together. Tony Salisbury, thank you. So many folks that every single day in Washington, I hope you know, are working hard to run as fast as you are to stand alongside you in this mission. This is not a conference with flags so we can pat ourselves on the back. I can tell you that if I sold that to President Trump as the objective, he’d kick me out of his office. This is an operational conference to bring our countries more closely together to achieve a shared objective and do so aggressively. This is not a one-way street. Every partner in this region has to do more and invest more in your security as well. We, like you, want a hemisphere of sovereign, secure, and prosperous nations. We, like you, want what another great American president, Teddy Roosevelt, called the permanent peace on this hemisphere, and it will require action from all of us. You know, our schools used to teach American school children about our blessed history. Every child in grade school grew up knowing the remarkable story of this country. They knew that President James Monroe was once an orphan. That at a mere 16 years old he had to look after his siblings. I’ve got an almost 16-year-old; he couldn’t look after anything. It’s remarkable. Well, that orphan grew to be not just one of the greatest presidents but one of the greatest Americans. After his presidency, when his wife died and he became frail and ill with heart failure, he still spoke about what he called our shared cause of liberty. He still talked about the toils and the perils of our war for independence. James Monroe, our fifth president, died on July 4th, Independence Day, 1831. 50 years after 1776. What I like to say is here at the Department of War, we're in the 1775 business, which is when Americans took up arms before they even declared independence. All of you are also in the 1775 business. Without the force of arms, without our militaries, we cannot keep our country safe. That’s my responsibility and it is yours. With Donald Trump in the Oval Office and with all of you here, we can still realize that long-ago dream of James Monroe in our time. We will make the Americas great again. Thank you and God bless you.

Sec. Pete Hegseth Hosts The Americas Counter Cartel Conference In Miami, Florida

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·112:48 ·1.9K views · 2026-03-05
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of War)
Secretary of War Pete Hegseth and SOUTHCOM leadership established a new "Donroe Doctrine" paradigm, shifting the regional response to cartels from a law enforcement/NGO model to a military-led offensive aimed at restoring national sovereignty and dis...
TL;DR Secretary of War Pete Hegseth and SOUTHCOM leadership established a new "Donroe Doctrine" paradigm, shifting the regional response to cartels from a law enforcement/NGO model to a military-led offensive aimed at restoring national sovereignty and dismantling narcoterroist...

TL;DR

Secretary of War Pete Hegseth and SOUTHCOM leadership established a new "Donroe Doctrine" paradigm, shifting the regional response to cartels from a law enforcement/NGO model to a military-led offensive aimed at restoring national sovereignty and dismantling narcoterroist networks through shared hemispheric burden-sharing.

The "Greater North America" Strategic Shift

  • The Monroe Doctrine Revived: Secretary Pete Hegseth and Senior Advisor Stephen Miller announced a return to the Monroe Doctrine, framing the Americas not as a separate region, but as an immediate extension of U.S. homeland defense.
      • Strategic Mapping: Hegseth introduced the concept of "Greater North America," defining the U.S. security perimeter as everything north of the equator (Greenland to Ecuador).
      • Quarter-Sphere Defense: For nations south of the equator, the U.S. seeks a "Quarter-Sphere Defense" model based on increased burden-sharing to secure the South Atlantic and South Pacific.
  • Military Primacy: The administration is pivoting away from what Stephen Miller described as "benign neglect" and "soft" NGO-led advice.
      • Lethal Force: Miller categorized cartels as the "ISIS and Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere," asserting they require a military solution rather than a purely criminal justice approach.
      • Restoring the Warrior Ethos: Hegseth explicitly rejected the phrase "diversity is our strength" in a military context, stating that "unity of purpose" and "shared heritage" are the true metrics of military effectiveness.

Offensive Operations and Operational Control

  • Deterrence through Action: General Francis Donovan and Secretary Hegseth highlighted current military operations as proof of a new, aggressive posture.
      • Operation Southern Spear: A kinetic maritime effort that has significantly reduced drug flows; Hegseth noted a recent period where no targets were found because narcoterroists were deterred from launching vessels.
      • Operation Absolute Resolve: Cited as a high-stakes raid in Caracas to arrest indicted fugitives, demonstrating U.S. willingness to act "with, by, through" partners or alone if necessary.
  • U.S. Border Militarization: Hegseth confirmed that the 101st Airborne Division is currently in operational control of the U.S. southern border, treating mass migration as a core military function rather than a civilian law enforcement issue.
  • Impact Metrics: The Department reported a 56% drop in fentanyl flows and a 20% plummet in nationwide murders following the implementation of these offensive strategies.

Sovereignty, Borders, and the Migration Cycle

  • Defining Sovereignty: Acting Assistant Secretary Joseph Humire argued that sovereignty is the "bedrock of human flourishing" and cannot exist without secure borders.
      • Localizing the Threat: The strategy aims to "remove the transnational" from transnational organized crime; by sealing borders, these groups become local, solvable problems rather than regional crises.
  • The "Vicious Cycle" of Migration: Stephen Miller detailed how lack of security leads to illegal immigration, which enriches cartels ($13 billion industry in 2022), which in turn further destabilizes partner nations by draining their human capital.
  • Prison and Territorial Control: Miller urged partner nations to reclaim every "square mile" of territory from paramilitary organizations, arguing that order must precede economic or cultural growth.

Partner Nation Perspectives and Requirements

  • El Salvador's Model: Minister Merino highlighted President Bukele’s success in transforming El Salvador from the "homicide capital" to the safest country in the hemisphere by prioritizing state force over NGO-led human rights critiques.
      • Naval Success: Reported a recent seizure of 6.6 tons of cocaine 380 miles off the coast.
  • Strategic Needs: Several nations, including Bolivia and Trinidad and Tobago, requested technological support and assets rather than "white papers."
      • Intelligence & Hardware: Partners cited needs for satellites, drones, scanners for trade ports, and maritime patrol vessels to bridge the "resource capacity gap."
  • Geopolitical Alignment:
      • Argentina: General Presti reaffirmed Argentina’s commitment under President Milei to act as a natural axis for South Atlantic stability.
      • Guyana & Jamaica: Both nations emphasized the link between drug cartels and the illegal small arms trade, with Jamaica reporting the seizure of 1,200 illegal weapons in 2025 alone.

Notable Quotes

"The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations."

— Stephen Miller, Assistant to the President for Homeland Security

"One of the dumbest phrases in military history... was 'Our diversity is our strength.' It’s the single dumbest phrase... because our strength is our unity. Our strength is in our shared purpose."

— Pete Hegseth, Secretary of War

"The person who forgives the wolf sacrifices the sheep... The state cannot be weaker than crime, and when crime takes over a state, it’s because its government officers are not in capacity to fight them because they are corrupt."

— Minister Merino, Minister of Defense of El Salvador

"We are not going to cede an inch of territory in this hemisphere to our enemies or adversaries. Our national security, our homeland security, the safety and well-being of our people begins at home, begins in our neighborhood."

— Stephen Miller, Assistant to the President for Homeland Security

Good morning. Welcome to the Americas Counter Cartel Conference, hosted by the Secretary of War, the Honorable Pete Hegseth, and the commander of the United States Southern Command, General Francis Donovan. We thank you for traveling to be here with us for this historic convening. The Americas Counter Cartel Conference is a testament to our partnership and this administration's unwavering commitment to secure the US homelands and our hemisphere. At this time, I'd like to introduce the commander of the United States Southern Command, General Francis Donovan. Buenos días. Welcome to Doral and the United States Southern Command for the Americas Counter Cartel Conference. It was a pleasure to meet many of you last night at the reception, and even better to see many of the friends and teammates we met last month at the CHODS conference in Washington D.C. Secretary Hegseth, sir, thank you for hosting this vital gathering and your enduring leadership. Each day you're ensuring the security, stability, and prosperity of the United States and the entire hemisphere. I'd also like to welcome and thank the Deputy Chief of Staff for Policy and Assistant to the President of Homeland Security, Mr. Stephen Miller. I also welcome the Under Secretary of War for Policy, the Honorable Eldridge Colby, who'll be joining us virtually today; the Chief of the National Guard Bureau, General Stephen Nordhaus—Spock, thank you very much for being here; and the Assistant Secretary of War for Cyber Policy, the Honorable Catherine Sutton. I also welcome the Acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas Security Affairs, and my now best friend, Mr. Joseph Humire; the Principal Deputy Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas Security Affairs, Mr. Marc DiBlasin; and the Senior Advisor to the Secretary of War, Mr. Eric Garesy, Mr. Patrick Weaver, and Mr. Phillip Hegseth. Welcome, most importantly, to all of our partner nation's ministers, ambassadors, and leaders joining us today. Thank you all for being here. Today we stand together at a very critical moment. Narco-traffickers and drug cartels engage in campaigns of terror, violence, and corruption to enrich themselves and expand their insidious influence. They are poisoning and killing all of our nation's citizens. These sophisticated networks flood our streets with fentanyl, cocaine, and other drugs that kill at a higher rate than any conventional war. This is the single gravest threat to security in our region and across our hemisphere. To counter this threat, the United States is guided by the strategic vision of peace through security set forth in both the United States National Security Strategy and the National Defense Strategy. These documents give our military a clear mandate in the Western Hemisphere to defend the US homeland and increase burden-sharing with our allies and partners to do so. We are your premier partner to work with, by, with, and through your nations to achieve shared objectives. But when necessary, we will not hesitate to act. Operation Southern Spear and Absolute Resolve are proof of this commitment. Through these efforts, the United States is combatting narcoterroism and drug trafficking, and most importantly, restoring deterrence. The Western Hemisphere is our neighborhood, and the narcoterroists who seek to export drugs and death to our shores and your shores will be dismantled and defeated. As allies and partners, we must take aggressive action together, sharing the burden through cooperation. We now call upon our long-standing relationships built on a foundation of trust and linked through shared culture, history, and economic aspirations to unite for the benefit of all of our nations. Our mutual security is at stake. Through combined operations, training exercises, and intelligence and information sharing, we will achieve greater domain awareness, interoperability, and capacity to strengthen regional security. With all nations shouldering this burden, our collective resilience will prevent shared adversaries from using the region to further their malign agendas. The time is now. Today marks the next step towards moving out to deter and defeat those who threaten the security, stability, and prosperity of the United States and this hemisphere. We all face these threats, and the most effective way to counter and eradicate them is by standing together. Before I turn the stage over to Mr. Humire, I'd like to once again thank you for your stalwart partnership. Your participation here sends an unmistakable message to partners and adversaries alike. We are taking the fight to the narcoterroists and the cartels. Not only are we redoubling our commitment to defend the sovereignty and security of our nations, we are doing it together to realize our common vision of a safer, stronger, and more prosperous Western Hemisphere. Let's get to work. Thank you, General Donovan. Ladies and gentlemen, performing the duties of Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and America's Security Affairs, Joseph Humire. Good morning. Buenos días. And I'd be remiss if I didn't begin by thanking my new best friend, General Donovan, for your excellent remarks and frankly for your excellent teamwork here at SOUTHCOM. Your team has truly been amazing at putting all this together in just a matter of few weeks. So thank you, General. I'd also like to begin by acknowledging a special team of senior advisors and defense officials who, if not for their efforts, not just today but over the last several months, we frankly just wouldn't be here today. So a special thank you to Principal Deputy Assistant Secretary DiBlasin, Acting Deputy Assistant Secretary Strasba, Mr. Eric Garesy, Senior Advisor Mateo Haydar, and a special sincere and heartfelt thank you to Senior Advisors Phillip Hegseth and Patrick Weaver. If it wasn't for you, frankly, this would not have happened. To all our interagency friends and foreign partners, this is truly a historic moment for the United States, for the Western Hemisphere, and for the world. In the room today, we have senior leadership from the Department of State, Department of Homeland Security, National Security Council, Homeland Security Council, Office of National Drug Control Policy, Office of the Vice President, and of course, the White House. I'm honored to be here with you today as Secretary Hegseth's Acting Assistant Secretary of War for Homeland Defense and the Americas. Welcome everyone to the first Americas Counter Cartel Conference in history. Every morning before I enter the Pentagon, I say a prayer in my car to our Heavenly Father. And I do this because I'm blessed. I'm blessed to be able to work in a once-in-a-lifetime administration, to work for a once-in-a-generation leader, President Donald J. Trump. And frankly, if it wasn't for President Trump, none of us, including our foreign partners, would be here today. President Trump, Secretary Hegseth, Mr. Stephen Miller—our leadership today understands an empirical truth that all the leadership of the United States missed for decades, if not centuries. They missed the fact that the Americas is an extension of US homeland defense, that hemispheric defense is a part of US homeland defense. And this statement of fact is now reflected in US policy through the 2025 National Security Strategy and the 2026 National Defense Strategy that correctly places the Americas as a regional priority. Just think about where we were a little over one year ago today. And those of you that know me know that I've been working and studying on regional issues for more than 20 years, so I can confidently say that in 2024, prior to President Trump, I had never seen the Americas in worse shape. Prior to President Trump, there was record-breaking illegal mass migration that was collapsing our border and many of your borders as well. There was record-breaking deaths of Americans from deadly drugs that were transiting through your countries and stealing your sovereignty. The explosion of transnational organized crime in every single country throughout the Americas was destroying our shared region. If you think about it, before President Trump, we were breaking all the records for all the wrong things. And it was even worse because there was a growing false narrative throughout the region that was taking hold that said you cannot win a war against major cartels and gangs because they are too powerful. In some cases, and even in some countries, frankly, they were giving up and adopted a posture of: if you can't beat them, join them. That's not tolerable anymore. Not for the United States, not for your countries, and not for our neighborhood. If you look to your right and you look to your left, you will find partners that are willing to fight with you. And if we collectively fight together, there is no gang, there is no terrorist, there is no cartel that is more powerful than us. We are here today because each of you has committed to doing your part. And that commitment cannot just be lip service. This must come with a new approach and a new normal to dismantle and destroy our shared threats of cartels and narcoterroism. There'll be a lot of discussions today in a myriad of topics, but I really want to quickly emphasize two that are central to this effort: border security and deterrence. If you do not control your borders, you do not have a country. Border security and territorial integrity, as President Trump has shown, is possible. Since 1648, since the Peace of Westphalia, sovereignty has been the bedrock of human flourishing. You do not have a democracy if you do not have your sovereignty, and you cannot have sovereignty without borders. Open borders make transnational organized crime fundamentally transnational. And you do not combat transnational crime with transnational institutions; you fight it with borders and national sovereignty. If we remove the transnational from transnational organized crime, it becomes a local problem, becomes a solvable problem. The second point is on deterrence. Interdiction, while necessary, is not deterrence. Dialogue, while useful, is not deterrence. Failing to control your prisons, your borders, or your waters is not deterrence. Going on offense is deterrence. Operation Southern Spear shows that deterrence is taking lethal and bold actions, and our goal is for every partner in this room today to start leading deterrence-focused offensive operations against your cartels. And finally, to wrap up, I want to make a point about geography. Geography in this hemisphere is indispensable to security. I mean, the secret sauce of Latin America and the Caribbean to be a regional priority for the United States, it's not the size of your trade, it's not your populations or your military, it's because we will always going to be connected because of our shared geography. The United States understood this going back to 1815, when two leaders in a relatively young Department of War pushed back against their own establishment at that time, not to look north or to look east, but rather to look south. The Department of War back then fought and won the Battle of New Orleans in 1815 that connected the United States to what is now the Gulf of America. In that moment, 210 years ago, connected my country to yours. And just a few years after that, these same two leaders led the Department of War to fight another war on our southern border, the Seminole Wars. This time to protect our borders against Indian tribes that trespassed our land, raided our towns, and killed Americans. We ultimately won these battles and transferred the great state of Florida in 1821, and then a mere two years later, one of these leaders declared something called the Monroe Doctrine. The legacy of then Secretary of War James Monroe, who later became President James Monroe, and Major General Andrew Jackson, who also later became president, defines the challenge that we have before us today. They say history doesn't repeat itself, but it tends to rhyme. And our interest over this shared geography is the same today as it was 200 years ago. Today, the Department of War once again has historic leadership under Secretary Pete Hegseth, committed to working with all of you to secure the Western Hemisphere once and for all. Thank you. God bless you. Thank you. I would now like to invite Deputy Chief of Staff for Policy and Assistant to the President for Homeland Security Stephen Miller to the podium. Sir, the floor is yours. Thank you, General Nordhaus. And thank you to my good friend, Secretary Hegseth, who I've long admired but I've gotten to know personally in the last number of months, and he is as fine a gentleman and as fierce a warrior as you will ever meet. And we've all learned to put our faith and confidence in his leadership of the War Department, so thank you, Pete. There's been a paradigm shift under President Trump in the Western Hemisphere in the last 14 months. Some of that was touched on by the earlier speakers, but just to restate it: we are not going to cede an inch of territory in this hemisphere to our enemies or adversaries. Our national security, our homeland security, the safety and well-being of our people begins at home, begins in our neighborhood, begins in our home region. And for too long, we have allowed foreign enemies, foreign adversaries, and enemy terrorist organizations to control territory and spaces in this hemisphere where they can project power, project threats, and directly threaten the lives of your citizens and our citizens. And so under the leadership of President Trump, we are using hard power, military power, lethal force to protect and defend the American homeland. Not a single one of your nations should tolerate the existence of a single square mile of territory that is under the control of any entity other than the sovereign governments of your country. The idea that we would have in this hemisphere areas that are under the physical control of foreign terrorist organizations, paramilitary organizations, is completely unacceptable. The cartels that operate in this hemisphere are the ISIS and the Al-Qaeda of the Western Hemisphere and should be treated just as brutally and just as ruthlessly as we treat those organizations. What we have learned after decades of effort is that there's not a criminal justice solution to the cartel problem. There are elements of the problem that require a criminal justice solution, to be sure, but just as we fought Al-Qaeda and fought ISIS with the tip of a very lethal spear, the reason why this is a conference with military leadership and not a conference of lawyers is because these organizations can only be defeated with military power. I see some heads nodding up front because they understand. You're dealing with a lot of lawyers in your own country, I'm sure. You have my permission not to listen to them. The key condition of human flourishing, of prosperity, is order and security. A country cannot pursue economic growth, cultural enrichment, art, architecture, education, science, if it doesn't have fundamental security first. And for too long we've been caught in a vicious cycle in this hemisphere, where countries from Mexico down to the tip of South America do not provide their citizens with basic physical security. So those citizens, in search of economic opportunity, illegally immigrate to the United States. They pay the smugglers, they pay the cartels, who then grow richer, who then grow more powerful. And then your countries in turn become poor because you've lost your citizens, you've lost your human capital, you've lost your opportunities to develop and to grow. You get poor, the cartels get more powerful, nothing changes, nothing improves. You have to break that cycle. You have to completely sever it. And that begins by dismantling these organizations that deal drugs, deal violence, assassinate, extort, blackmail, and also traffic illegally in human beings. That is a form of terrorism too. Illegal immigration facilitated by criminal networks is a form of terrorism. And those who are stealing your children and stealing your families and stealing your human capital are part of that terrorist continuum and should be thought of as such. It is not a victimless crime when people are paying human smuggling organizations, because that money then fuels assassinations, it fuels violence, it fuels instability, and it continues this cycle. For decades, a lot of organizations were sent to your countries—these are known as NGOs, I think you know the term, non-governmental organizations—and they gave you very, very bad advice for a very, very long time. Basically they told you, in short, that you needed to be softer and gentler and nicer to the criminals, and that you had to demilitarize your approach and extend more rights and more opportunities to the people who are sabotaging and destroying your society. I would make the point to you, as others have pointed out before, including the president of El Salvador, that the West only achieved the place that it did in human history because it spent previous centuries eradicating the criminal elements within its territories. So the West that we came to know, that had the great music and architecture and science and the most powerful economy, it had spent centuries previous to that establishing order first. So that by the time you get to, you know, say 18th century England, 19th century England, there had been centuries of previous history in which ruthless means were used to get rid of the people who were raping and murdering and defying established systems of order and justice. And then when people could live freely and peaceably and children and families and women could be safe, then it became possible to pursue all of these higher elements of life. So when people who are coming to countries like yours that have had struggles and difficulties and pains and challenges and they're telling you anything other than first and foremost secure your countries and your territory, they are giving you not only terrible advice, but advice that if someone had come to Europe centuries ago and they had followed that advice, Europe would have never become the Europe that it eventually became. The reason why many Western countries are struggling today is they've forgotten the eternal truth and wisdom they once followed.
